The Contradiction of Accepting Imagination in Theology
Razi refutes two groups by exposing self-contradiction when imagination is used.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi exposes the logical contradiction that arises when both anthropomorphists and materialists rely on imagination in their arguments about God, and why the correct approach is to exclude imagination from divine matters.

Core Idea
Accepting imagination forces both groups into self-contradiction.

Imam al-Razi shows that both the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) and the materialists (al-dahriyya) rely on imagination to reason about God, but their own positions are internally inconsistent. This lesson walks through his elegant refutation and the correct method of transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi builds his argument step by step.
Focus on the conditional structure: 'If imagination is accepted... then...'
2
Identify the two groups and their claims
Find where Razi states the claims of the anthropomorphists and the materialists. What do they each say about God's relationship to the world?
Anthropomorphists: God must be in a direction. Materialists: God must be prior in time.
3
Understand the contradiction
Razi argues that if imagination is accepted, each group is forced to adopt the other's position. Work through the logic to see why this is a contradiction.
This is the heart of the refutation: consistency would require each to affirm what they deny.
4
Learn the correct method
Razi concludes that the only consistent position is to reject imagination altogether. Understand what 'ahl al-tawhid wa al-tanzih' means here.
This is the positive doctrine: God is beyond all imaginative categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محالان. فثبت : أن الباري قبل العالم . ثم قالوا : والعلم الضروري حاصل. بأن هذه القبلية لا تكون إلا بالزمان والمدة . وإذا ثبت هذا فتقدم الباري على العالم إن كان بمدة متناهية ، لزم حدوث الباري . وإن كان بمدة لا أول لها ، لزم كون المدة قديمة . فأنتجوا بهذا الطريق: قدم المدة والزمان. فنقول : حاصل هذا الكلام : أن المشبهة زعمت أن مباينة الباري تعالى عن العالم ، لا يعقل حصولها إلا بالجهة . وأنتجوا منه : كون الإله في الجهة. وزعمت الدهرية : أن تقدم الباري تعالى على العالم، لا يعقل حصوله إلا بالزمان . وأنتجوا منه : قدم المدة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : حكم الخيال في حق الله تعالى ، إما أن يكون مقبولا أو غير مقبول . فإن كان مقبولا ، فالمشبهة يلزم عليهم مذهب الدهرية ، وهو أن يكون الباري تعالى متقدما على العالم بمدة غير متناهية ، ويلزمهم القول بكون الزمان أزلياً . والمشبهة لا يقولون بذلك . والدهرية يلزم عليهم مذهب المشبهة . وهو مباينة الباري تعالى عن العالم بالجهة والمكان فيلزمهم القول بكون الباري تعالى مكانيا - وهم لا يقولون به - فصار هذا التناقض واردا على الفريقين . و أما إن قلنا : حكم الوهم والخيال غير مقبول البتة في ذات الله تعالى وفي صفاته ، فحينئذ تقول : قول المشبهة : إن كل موجودين فلابد وأن يكون أحدهما حالا في الآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة : قول خيالي باطل ، وقول الدهرية بأن تقدم الباري تعالى على العالم ، لا بد وأن يكون بالمدة والزمان : قول خيالي باطل. وذلك هو قول أصحابنا أهل التوحيد والتنزيه ، الذين عزلوا حكم الوهم والخيال عن ذات الله تعالى وصفاته. وذلك هو المنهج القويم، والصراط المستقيم .
Impossible. So it is established that the Creator (al-Bari) is before the world. Then they said: Necessary knowledge is obtained that this priority is only by time (zaman) and duration (mudda). And if this is established, then the priority of the Creator over the world: if it is by a finite duration, it entails the temporality (huduth) of the Creator; and if it is by a duration that has no beginning, it entails that the duration is eternal (qadim). So they inferred by this path: the eternity of duration and time. We say: The summary of this speech is that the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) claimed that the distinction of the Creator, exalted is He, from the world is only conceivable by direction (jiha). And they inferred from it: that God is in a direction. And the materialists (al-dahriyya) claimed that the priority of the Creator over the world is only conceivable by time. And they inferred from it: the eternity of duration. And when this is established, we say: The judgment of imagination (al-khayal) regarding God, exalted is He, is either accepted or not accepted. So if it is accepted, then the anthropomorphists are compelled to adopt the doctrine of the materialists, which is that the Creator is prior to the world by an infinite duration, and they are compelled to say that time is eternal. But the anthropomorphists do not say that. And the materialists are compelled to adopt the doctrine of the anthropomorphists, which is the distinction of the Creator from the world by direction and place, so they are compelled to say that the Creator is spatial (makani). But they do not say that. So this contradiction applies to both groups. But if we say: The judgment of imagination and fantasy (al-wahm) is absolutely not accepted regarding the essence of God, exalted is He, and His attributes, then we say: The statement of the anthropomorphists - that every two existents must either be one in the other or distinct by direction - is a false imaginative statement. And the statement of the materialists - that the priority of the Creator over the world must be by duration and time - is a false imaginative statement. That is the statement of our companions, the people of monotheism and transcendence (ahl al-tawhid wa al-tanzih), who excluded the judgment of imagination and fantasy from the essence of God and His attributes. And that is the upright path and the straight way.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups that rely on imagination in theological matters: the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) and the materialists (al-dahriyya). Imam al-Razi presents their respective arguments and then exposes a contradiction if their method of accepting imaginative judgments (hukm al-khayal) is applied consistently. The anthropomorphists claim that any distinction between two existents must be either by inherence (hulul) or by spatial direction (jiha). From this they infer that God must be in a direction. The materialists claim that the priority of the Creator over the world must be temporal (bi al-zaman wa al-mudda). From this they infer the eternity of time and duration. Razi then argues: if the judgment of imagination is accepted in God, then the anthropomorphists are forced to admit that God's priority over the world is by an infinite duration (as they have no other way to conceive priority), which implies the eternity of time—a view they reject. Similarly, the materialists are forced to admit spatial distinction for God, which implies that God is in a place—a view they reject. Thus, both groups contradict themselves. The correct position, according to Ahl al-Tawhid, is to reject the validity of imagination and fantasy altogether in matters of God's essence and attributes. This is the sound methodology.

Key Terms
المشبهة
الدهرية
الوهم
الخيال
المباينة
الجهة
الزمان
المدة
التناقض
التوحيد
التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the refutation is only ad hominem against these two groups, not establishing a universal principle.
Correction: Razi is establishing a universal principle that imagination has no authority in theology; the contradiction shows the inconsistency of using it.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, what happens if one accepts the judgments of imagination (al-khayal) in God's essence?
A) One ends up in a contradiction between the positions of the anthropomorphists and materialists.
B) One correctly affirms divine transcendence (tanzih).
C) One must affirm temporal succession in God.
D) One must deny creation ex nihilo.

Key Takeaway
Imagination has no authority in theology; rejecting it avoids contradiction and upholds divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely heard or used statements like 'Allah is above the heavens' or 'Allah is with us.' Without realizing it, do you think you are falling into the error of al-mushabbiha by imagining a direction? This lesson forces you to examine your own understanding: will you now commit to not letting your imagination dictate your theology?
?
Consider the materialist's error of making time a necessary condition for priority. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that actually impose created limitations on Him, such as assuming that His actions must be in time? This lesson demands that you identify and abandon any such assumption.
?
The lesson ends with 'the upright path' being to exclude imagination from God's essence and attributes. What intellectual habits do you need to change today to walk that path? Is there a specific attribute of Allah that you habitually imagine in a human-like way? Write down one and commit to correcting it using revelation alone.

The Tenth Point: Why Knowledge of God's Actions is More Accessible
Razi introduces the tenth point that knowledge of God's actions and attributes is more accessible than knowledge of His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that, according to Imam Razi, knowledge of God's actions and attributes is more accessible to human reason than knowledge of His essence, and that this understanding requires accepting what contradicts sensory experience.

Core Idea
What we never observe, we can still affirm of God.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the tenth point of his argument for divine transcendence. He argues that it is easier for human intellects to understand what God does than what God is. Even anthropomorphists, who tend to liken God to creation, agree that God's actions often go against how we normally perceive things. To prove this, Razi gives a striking example involving creation out of nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim followed by an example.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where Razi states that knowledge of God's actions/attributes is 'closer to intellects' than knowledge of His essence. Note that even anthropomorphists agree that such knowledge contradicts sense and imagination.
This is the core thesis of the tenth point.
3
Examine the example
Razi gives one example: we observe transformations (water/earth to plants, plants to animal parts), but we have never observed direct creation of animals from nothing. Yet we affirm God did that. Why does this support his claim?
Consider how this contrasts with sensory experience.
4
Reflect on the implication
Since we accept that God's actions defy observation and imagination for this one case, we should be ready to accept that His essence also defies imagination. This is the method of tanzih (transcendence).
5
Summarize in your own words
Write one sentence that captures the lesson's core: what we affirm about God's actions may contradict our senses, and that's okay—it shows His essence is beyond our imagination.
This will help solidify the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن معرفة أفعال الله تعالى وصفاته ، أقرب إلى العقول ، من معرفة ذات الله تعالى . ثم المشبهة وافقونا على أن معرفة أفعال الله تعالى وصفاته ، على خلاف حكم الحس والخيال . أما تقرير هذا المعنى في أفعال الله تعالى فذلك من وجوه : أحدها : إن الذي شاهدناه هو تغير الصفات ، مثل انقلاب الماء والتراب نباتا ، وانقلاب النبات جزء بدن حيوان . فأما حدوث الدواب ابتداء ، من غير سبق مادة وطينة : فهذا شيء ما شاهدناه البتة ، ولا يقضى بجوازه وهمنا وخيالنا ، مع أنا سلمنا أنه تعالى هو المحدث للدواب ابتداء ، من غير سبق مادة وطينة
The tenth: Knowledge of the actions of God, exalted is He, and His attributes is closer to intellects than knowledge of the essence of God, exalted is He. Then the anthropomorphists agree with us that knowledge of the actions of God and His attributes is contrary to the judgment of sense and imagination. As for the exposition of this meaning regarding the actions of God, it is from aspects: One of them: What we have observed is change of qualities, such as the transformation of water and earth into plants, and the transformation of plants into part of the body of an animal. As for the origination of animals (al-dawab) initially, without preceding matter and clay: this is something we have never observed, nor does our imagination (wahmuna wa khayaluna) judge its permissibility, despite that we affirm that He, exalted is He, is the originator of animals initially, without preceding matter and clay.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage introduces the tenth point of a larger argument. Razi states that knowledge of God's actions and attributes is closer to human intellects than knowledge of His essence. He notes that even the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) agree that our understanding of God's actions and attributes often contradicts the judgments of sense perception and imagination (hukm al-hiss wa al-khayal). To illustrate this, Razi provides a series of examples. The first example (أحدها) is that we observe ordinary changes in qualities, like water and earth turning into plants, and plants becoming part of an animal's body. However, we have never observed the direct creation of animals ex nihilo (without pre-existing matter and clay). Our imagination does not accept such a thing as possible, yet we affirm that God did exactly that—He created animals initially without any prior material. This shows that we accept in God's actions what is contrary to sensory experience and imagination. Therefore, we should also be willing to accept transcendence (tanzih) in His essence, even if it defies imagination.

Key Terms
أفعال الله
صفاته
ذات الله
الحس
الخيال
الدواب
إحداث
مادة
طينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is denying the possibility of creation ex nihilo because imagination rejects it.
Correction: Razi is using the rejection by imagination as evidence that we should not rely on imagination for theological truths. He affirms that God did create animals from nothing, showing that divine actions can transcend sensory experience.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is one example of a divine action that contradicts sensory experience?
A) Transformation of water and earth into plants.
B) Direct creation of animals from nothing without matter.
C) The creation of human intellect.
D) God acting without purpose.

Key Takeaway
We must accept that many divine actions are beyond sensory experience, and this helps us understand that God's essence is also beyond imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God created animals from nothing, even though your imagination finds it impossible. Now, think of a recent event in your life that seemed unfair or meaningless. Did you trust that God's action was wise, or did you rely on your own imagination to judge it? What would it mean for you to truly embrace that God's ways are beyond your comprehension?
?
Is there an aspect of God's essence (such as His being free from spatial location or emotional change) that you struggle to accept because it feels distant or abstract? How does Razi's argument challenge you to submit to revelation even when your mind resists?
?
When you face a situation that defies your expectations, do you tend to demand an explanation from God, or do you humbly accept that His actions may contradict your senses and imagination? Write one concrete step you can take this week to practice trusting God's unseen wisdom.

Time Created Without Time
Razi gives the second example: time created without time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the example of time's creation without time to demonstrate that rational judgments can override sensory and imaginative assumptions.

Core Idea
Time's own creation has no temporal container.

In this lesson, we continue with Razi's four faces. The second face addresses a specific paradox: we think of all events as happening within time, yet we affirm that time itself was created. But if time was created, there was no time before its creation for it to occur in. Razi uses this to challenge our reliance on imagination in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Razi states two things: our ordinary conception of events in time, and our theological judgment about time itself.
2
Identify the apparent contradiction
Ask yourself: If we only conceive events in time, how can we affirm that time itself occurred without time? This seems contradictory to ordinary reasoning.
This is exactly the point Razi wants to highlight.
3
Reflect on the theological lesson
Consider why Razi uses this example. It shows that in matters of God and creation, we must follow rational theology even when it conflicts with sensory and imaginative habits.
This supports the broader argument of the Kitab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : إنا لا نعقل حدوث شيء وتكونه ، إلا في زمان مخصوص . ثم حكمنا بأن الزمان حدث لا في زمان البتة
And the second: We do not conceive the occurrence (huduth) and coming-to-be (takawwun) of anything except in a specific time. Then we judged that time (al-zaman) itself occurred (hadatha) not in any time at all.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This is the second of the four aspects (wujuh) by which Razi demonstrates that we accept in God's actions what contradicts sensory and imaginative judgments. Here he points to the concept of time. Human reason naturally conceives of any event or coming-into-existence as occurring within a specific time. However, in Islamic theology, time itself is a created entity (muhdath). We affirm that time began to exist, but its own origination cannot have occurred in a pre-existing time, because time did not exist before it was created. Thus, we judge that time was created 'not in any time at all,' even though our ordinary reasoning would require a temporal framework for any occurrence. This shows that our intellects can accept truths that defy the normal patterns of imagination (al-khayal).

Key Terms
حدوث
تكون
زمان
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time's creation must itself be within time, otherwise it is a logical contradiction.
Correction: Razi shows that this is not a contradiction but a truth accepted rationally; time's creation cannot be placed in time because time did not exist before its creation.

Checkpoint
What theological truth does Razi highlight in the second face?
A) Time is eternal.
B) Time was created within time.
C) Time was created not in any time.
D) Events occur outside time.

Key Takeaway
Time's own origination is not temporally conditioned, showing that rational theology can affirm truths beyond imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to imagine time before creation, do you feel the limits of your imagination? How does accepting that time itself was created without time challenge your reliance on your senses for understanding divine attributes? Will you trust your reason even when it goes beyond your imagination?
?
The passage says 'we judged' – are you personally willing to affirm a truth that your imagination cannot grasp? What other truths of aqeedah do you struggle with because they surpass your mental pictures? Commit today to submit your imagination to the authority of revelation and reason.
?
Razi points out that we accept time's creation without a temporal container. Does your heart truly submit to this truth, or do you still try to wrap it in a mental timeline? Will you let this lesson train you to accept what Allah reveals, even when you cannot picture it?

Acting Without Change
Razi gives the third example: we accept that God acts without undergoing change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's act of creation does not involve any change in His essence or attributes, contrary to human analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
God acts without changing — a key principle of Sunni theology.

In this third example from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis, we encounter a profound theological principle. Human reasoning normally assumes that when someone begins to act, they must have undergone some change — a shift in state or attribute. Yet we Muslims affirm that Allah created the world without any such change. This lesson explores that paradox and why it is central to our understanding of God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the contrast between human reasoning and divine action. Pay special attention to the phrase 'من غير شيء من ذلك'.
The phrase 'from any of that' indicates a negation of change and alteration.
2
Understand the human assumption
Razi points out that we naturally think an agent who starts acting must have changed. Identify this assumption in the text: the idea that acting requires a change in state or attribute.
This is an example of analogical reasoning from created beings.
3
Recognize the theological exception
We affirm that God created the world without any change. This shows that God is not subject to the limitations of created beings and that His action is not dependent on a prior change.
This aligns with the Sunni position of tanzih (transcendence).
4
Reflect on the bigger picture
Consider why this matters: it protects God's perfection and eternality. Our imagination (khayal) should not be applied to God in matters of theology.
Razi uses this to argue against using imaginative analogies for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : إنا لا نعقل فاعلا يفعل ، بعد ما لم يكن فاعلا ، إلا لتغير حالة و تبدل صفة ، ثم إنا اعترفنا بأنه تعالى خلق العالم من غير شيء من ذلك
And the third: We do not conceive an agent (fa'il) that does an act, after not being an agent, except due to a change in state and an alteration of attribute. Then we acknowledge that He, exalted is He, created the world without any of that.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
In this third example, Razi points out a common assumption in human reasoning: when an agent begins to act after not acting, we assume that the agent underwent some change in state or attribute. For instance, a person who was sitting and then stands up has changed. However, when it comes to God, we affirm that He created the world after not having created it, but He did so without any change in His essence or attributes. God is eternally powerful and willing; His act of creation does not imply a new attribute or alteration in Him. This is another instance where we accept a truth that contradicts our normal imaginative reasoning, reinforcing the principle that imagination (al-khayal) should not be applied to God.

Key Terms
فاعل
تغير حالة
تبدل صفة
خلق
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's action implies change in Him, similar to how created agents change when they act.
Correction: Razi clarifies that we affirm divine action without any change, because God is not subject to the limitations of creatures.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say we normally assume about an agent who begins to act?
A) That the agent must have a purpose.
B) That the agent undergoes a change in state or attribute.
C) That the agent must be in time.
D) That the agent must be spatial.

Key Takeaway
We affirm that God created the world without undergoing any change in His essence or attributes, a truth that transcends human imaginative reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah creating the universe, do you catch yourself picturing Him 'starting' to act, like a human craftsman? How does this lesson challenge that habit? Will you actively resist reducing Allah to your imagination next time you think of creation?
?
Have you ever felt uneasy about the idea that Allah's will was complete before creation, as if that makes creation somehow unnecessary? How does Razi's point free you from thinking that God 'changed' in order to create? What will you do when that doubt resurfaces?
?
Your mind naturally assumes that every action implies a change in the agent. But Allah's creation shatters that pattern. How comfortable are you with having your natural assumptions overturned by revelation? What other assumptions about Allah might you be clinging to that need to be surrendered?

4 min
Agent Acting Without Purpose
Razi contrasts human agency with divine agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the example of purposeful action to show that God's acts transcend ordinary human reasoning about motives.

Core Idea
God acts without any need for benefit or fear of harm.

In this fourth example, Razi highlights another ordinary human assumption: that every act is done for some benefit or to avoid harm. Yet we affirm that God created the world without any such motive. This lesson examines how this counter-intuitive truth demonstrates the need for a method of tanzih that goes beyond human analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi contrasts our ordinary understanding of agency with our belief about God.
The phrase 'without any of this' may be a scribal error; it means 'without any such motive'.
2
Identify the key components
The passage has two parts: (1) the general rule about created agents acting for benefit or harm, (2) our acknowledgment that God created the world without any such motive. Spot these two claims.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does Razi say this is an example of accepting something against our ordinary reasoning? How does it show that God is not bound by the same rules as creatures?
This connects to the core idea that God's actions are free from need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : إنا لا نعقل فاعلا يفعل فعلا ، إلا لجلب منفعة ، أو لدفع مضرة . ثم إنا اعترفنا : بأنه تعالى خالق العالم الغير شيء من هذا
And the fourth: We do not conceive an agent doing an act except for attracting a benefit (manfa'a) or repelling a harm (madarra). Then we acknowledge that He, exalted is He, is the Creator of the world without any of this.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This is the fourth and final example in Razi's series of aspects demonstrating that we accept truths about God that defy ordinary human reasoning. Here he points to the motivation behind actions. In our ordinary experience, when someone performs an action, it is typically to achieve some benefit or avoid some harm. This is how we understand agency in created beings. However, when it comes to God, we affirm that He created the world without any need for benefit or fear of harm. God is absolutely self-sufficient (ghani) and His actions are not motivated by any external purpose; rather, they issue from His wisdom and will without any deficiency or need. This again shows that our imaginative analogies from the created realm cannot be applied to the Creator. The acceptance of such truths requires a method of tanzih (transcendence) that sets aside imaginative judgments.

Key Terms
فاعل
فعل
منفعة
مضرة
خالق
عالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's actions must have a purpose in the sense of benefiting Him or avoiding harm.
Correction: Razi clarifies that God acts without any such motive; God is absolutely self-sufficient and His actions are not driven by need.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the usual motive for an agent's action in our experience?
A) To demonstrate power.
B) For a divine decree.
C) To attract benefit or repel harm.
D) To fulfill a command.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not motivated by benefit or harm, distinguishing Him from created agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you catch yourself seeking a personal benefit (like feeling good or avoiding punishment) instead of focusing on God's absolute self-sufficiency? How can you shift your intention to worship without any expectation of return?
?
If God does not need anything from you, then why do you sometimes act as if your obedience completes Him? What would it mean to truly internalize that God is independent of all your actions?
?
Think of a time you refrained from a sin only out of fear of harm. Does that make your action less pure? How can you train your soul to act solely out of reverence for God's transcendence, not from self-interest?

The Meaning of 'Ahad' Negates Corporeality and Substance
Razi explains how 'Ahad' as hyperbole negates corporeality and substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the name 'Ahad' as a hyperbole of oneness necessarily negates that Allah is a body or a substance, according to Imam Razi's argument.

Core Idea
The name 'Ahad' is not just 'one' — it is a radical oneness that excludes all composition and likeness.

Imam Razi now takes the second word of Surah al-Ikhlas, 'Ahad', and shows that this name, being an intensified form of oneness, cannot coexist with the notion of a body or a substance. He presents two lines of reasoning depending on one's view of the indivisible atom. This lesson unpacks those arguments and their implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi opens with a direct claim: 'Ahad' is a hyperbole of unity, so it contradicts corporeality. Then note that he splits the discussion for substance into two camps.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Examine the first argument against substance
Focus on the argument for those who deny the indivisible atom. Trace the logic: every extended thing has distinct sides → distinction implies divisibility → divisible things cannot be truly one → thus Allah, being Ahad, cannot be extended or a substance.
Razi uses the example of right and left.
3
Examine the second argument against substance
Now see the alternative argument for those who affirm the indivisible atom. Since Ahad also negates any equal or counterpart, if Allah were an individual substance, other atoms would be like Him, violating His uniqueness. The verse 'nor is there any comparable to Him' supports this.
This argument relies on the meaning of Ahad as excluding likeness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that Surah al-Ikhlas proves Allah is neither a body nor a substance. Consider how both arguments, despite different starting assumptions, converge on the same negation.
This shows the depth of the term Ahad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان قوله أحد مبالغة في الواحدية كان قوله أحد منافياً للجسمية. وأما دلالته على أنه ليس بجوهر فنقول: أما الذين ينكرون الجوهر الفرد فإنهم يقولون: إن كل متحيز فلابد وأن يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الثاني. وذلك لأنه لابد من أن يتميز يمينه عن يساره وقدامه عن خلفه وفوقه عن تحته. وكل ما يتميز فيه شيء عن شيء فهو منقسم، لأن يمينه موصوف بأنه يمين لا يسار، ويساره موصوف بأنه يسار لا يمين. فلو كان يمينه عين يساره لاجتمع في الشيء الواحد: أنه يمين وليس بيمين، ويسار وليس بيسار. فيلزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات في الشيء الواحد وهو محال. قالوا: فثبت أن كل متحيز فهو منقسم، وثبت أن كل منقسم فهو ليس بأحد. ولما كان الله سبحانه وتعالى موصوفاً بأنه أحد وجب أن لا يكون متحيزاً أصلاً، وذلك ينفي كونه جوهراً. وأما الذين يثبتون الجوهر الفرد فإنه لا يمكنهم الاستدلال على نفي كونه تعالى جوهرا من هذا الاعتبار، ويمكنهم أن يحتجوا بهذه الآية على نفي كونه جوهرا من وجه آخر وبيانه: هو أن الأحد كما يراد به نفي التركيب والتأليف في الذات فقد يراد به أيضا نفي الضد والند. فلو كان تعالى جوهرا فردا لكان كل جوهر فرد مثلا له وذلك ينفي كونه أحدا. ثم أكدوا هذا الوجه بقوله تعالى: {ولم يكن له كفوا أحد}. ولو كان جوهرا لكان كل جوهر فرد كفوا له. فدلت هذه السورة من الوجه الذي قررناه على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر.
And since His saying 'Ahad' is an exaggeration in oneness, His saying 'Ahad' is contradictory to corporeality. As for its indication that He is not a substance, we say: As for those who deny the indivisible atom, they say: Every extended thing must necessarily have one of its two sides distinguished from the second. That is because its right must be distinguished from its left, its front from its back, its above from its below. And everything in which something is distinguished from something is divisible, because its right is described as being right, not left, and its left is described as being left, not right. For if its right were identical to its left, then in that one thing it would be both right and not right, left and not left. This entails the combination of negation and affirmation in one thing, which is impossible. They said: So it is established that every extended thing is divisible, and it is established that everything divisible is not 'Ahad'. And since Allah, glorified and exalted, is described as 'Ahad', it is necessary that He is not extended at all, and that negates His being a substance. As for those who affirm the indivisible atom, they cannot deduce the negation of His being a substance from this consideration, but they can argue with this verse for the negation of His being a substance from another aspect. Its explanation is: 'Ahad', just as it is intended to negate composition and combination in the essence, so also it may be intended to negate the contrary and the equal. For if He, exalted, were an individual substance, then every individual substance would be similar to Him, and that would negate His being 'Ahad'. Then they strengthened this aspect by His saying, exalted: {And there is none comparable to Him.} And if He were a substance, then every individual substance would be comparable to Him. So this surah, from the aspect we have stated, indicates that He, exalted, is not a body and not a substance.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and proof passage where Imam Razi explains the theological implications of the divine name 'Ahad' (the One). He begins by stating that 'Ahad' is a hyperbole (mubalagha) of oneness (wahdaniyya), not just simple unity, and therefore it is incompatible with corporeality (jismiyya). Then he provides two lines of reasoning for negating that Allah is a substance (jawhar). The first is for those who deny the atom (jawhar fard): every extended (mutahayyiz) thing must have distinct sides, making it divisible, and whatever is divisible cannot be truly one (ahad). The second is for those who affirm the atom: they cannot use the same argument but can use the verse to argue that 'Ahad' also negates any equal or counterpart (nidd), and if Allah were an individual atom, every other atom would be like Him, violating His uniqueness. He concludes that the surah (al-Ikhlas) proves Allah is neither a body nor a substance.

الواحدية
الجسمية
جوهر
متحيز

الضد والند

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Ahad' simply means numerically one without implying anything about composition or likeness.
Correction: Razi shows that 'Ahad' is a hyperbole that necessarily negates corporeality, composition, and any equal or counterpart.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the name 'Ahad' negate that Allah is a body?
A) Because every body is divisible and 'Ahad' means indivisible.
B) Because 'Ahad' is a hyperbole of oneness that contradicts composition.
C) Because bodies require a place and direction.
D) Because 'Ahad' implies existence without cause.

Key Takeaway
The name 'Ahad' in Surah al-Ikhlas implies a radical oneness that excludes both composition (body) and substance (jawhar), affirming Allah's absolute uniqueness.

?
Razi presents two arguments: one based on divisibility, another on incomparability. Which of these exposes a hidden form of shirk in your own understanding of Allah's oneness? How will you actively replace that subtle error with the pure tawhid demanded by this surah?
?
If Allah is absolutely One with no parts, no sides, and no equal, then how must this reshape the way you approach Him in worship and du'a? Are there any ways you have been treating Allah as if He needs your worship or as if He is limited like a created being? What specific change will you make in your devotion to reflect His true oneness?

The Rational Proof from 'Huwa Allahu Ahad'
Razi presents a rational proof based on the meaning of 'Ilah' and composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi extracts a rational proof for God's absolute oneness and transcendence from the Quranic verse 'Huwa Allahu Ahad'.

Core Idea
The verse 'Huwa Allahu Ahad' contains a logical proof against composition.

Imam Razi, in his work Asas al-Taqdis, demonstrates that the Quran not only declares God's oneness but also provides the rational grounds for it. In this passage, he unpacks the verse 'Huwa Allahu Ahad' to show that the very meaning of 'Ilah' (God) implies independence, which precludes any form of composition. This lesson walks through that powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice how Razi starts with a premise about the meaning of 'Ilah'.
2
Identify the key premise
Identify the premise that 'Ilah' implies independence (ghina) and that every composite is dependent on its parts. These are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow how Razi concludes that God cannot be composite, therefore He is Ahad, and therefore He is not a body, substance, or in a place. See the logical chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه تعالى كما نص على أنه واحد فقد نص أيضا على البرهان الذي لأجله يجب الحكم بأنه أحد. وذلك أنه قال: {هو الله أحد}، وكونه إلها يقتضي كونه غنيا عما سواه، وكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره، فكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره، وكونه إلها يمنع من كونه مفتقرا إلى غيره. وذلك يوجب القطع بكونه أحدا. وكونه أحدا يوجب القطع بأنه ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا في حيز وجهة. فثبت أن قوله تعالى: {وهو الله أحد} برهان قاطع على ثبوت هذه المطالب.
And know that He, exalted, just as He stated that He is One, He also stated the proof by which it is necessary to judge that He is Ahad. That is because He said: 'He is Allah, Ahad.' And His being Ilah necessitates His being independent of everything else. And every composite is dependent upon each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it. So every composite is dependent upon other than itself. And His being Ilah prevents His being dependent upon other than Himself. That necessitates the certainty of His being Ahad. And His being Ahad necessitates the certainty that He is not a body, nor a substance, nor in a space or direction. So it is established that His saying, exalted, 'And He is Allah, Ahad' is a decisive proof for the establishment of these claims.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a proof passage where Imam Razi presents a rational argument derived directly from the Quranic verse 'Huwa Allahu Ahad'. He points out that the verse contains both the declaration of oneness and the intrinsic proof for it. The argument proceeds as follows: The name 'Ilah' (God) implies being self-sufficient (ghani) and independent of everything else. However, any composite (murakkab) thing depends on its parts, which are other than itself. Therefore, anything composite is dependent on something other than itself. Since God cannot be dependent, He cannot be composite. This entails that He is Ahad (absolutely one). From that, it follows necessarily that He is not a body, not a substance, and not in space or direction. Razi calls this a 'decisive proof' (burhan qati') that establishes all these attributes of transcendence.

Key Terms
إله
غني
مركب
مفتقر
أحد
برهان قاطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse 'Huwa Allahu Ahad' merely states God's oneness without providing a logical argument.
Correction: Razi shows that the verse contains an implicit rational proof by linking 'Ilah' to independence and negating composition.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, how does the verse 'Huwa Allahu Ahad' contain a logical proof against composition?
A) The word 'Ahad' itself means indivisible.
B) 'Allah' implies sovereignty, and sovereignty excludes composition.
C) Being 'Ilah' entails independence, and composition entails dependence on parts.
D) The verse appears after earlier verses negating partner.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse 'Huwa Allahu Ahad' is a decisive rational proof for God's absolute oneness and transcendence over body, substance, and space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' daily. But do you truly believe that God is absolutely independent of everything—including your worship? Does your heart rely on Him alone, or do you still subconsciously depend on created means as if they have power? Let this verse strip away your false dependencies.
?
The verse proves God is not in a space or direction. Yet, when you make du'a, do you feel you are speaking to someone 'up there'? Does your imagination of God still place Him in a direction? Confront this and let your belief be purified. What will you change about your mindfulness of God's presence?

The Meaning of 'al-Samad' and the First Proof Against Corporeality
Razi defines 'al-Samad' and presents the first proof against corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical meaning of the divine name 'al-Samad' and how the first rational argument from it disproves corporeality.

Core Idea
'Al-Samad' means the Master to whom all needs are directed, which immediately negates any need for parts or location.

Razi begins his proof from the Qur'anic name 'al-Samad' by first establishing its meaning. He then draws an immediate implication: this name indicates that Allah is not a body and not confined to space. He then launches into a detailed argument; the first face of that argument shows that every body is composite and therefore dependent. This lesson covers that foundational step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the text's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice that the passage ends mid-argument; only the first proof is present.
2
Understand the meaning of al-Samad
Razi defines al-Samad as 'the master to whom one turns in needs' (al-sayyid al-masmud ilayhi fi al-hawa'ij). Reflect on how this meaning implies independence and being the ultimate resort.
This meaning itself already negates corporeality and spatial confinement.
3
Follow the first proof against corporeality
Trace the logical chain: every body is composite → every composite needs each of its parts → each part is other than itself → therefore every composite needs something other than itself. Ask: how does this relate to 'al-Samad'?
The full argument would conclude that Allah, being al-Samad, is not a body because bodies are dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله سبحانه وتعالى: {الله الصمد}، فالصمد هو السيد المصمود إليه في الحوائج، وذلك يدل على أنه ليس بجسم وعلى أنه غير مختص بالحيز والجهة. وأما بيان دلالته على ففي الجسمية فمن وجوه: الأول: إن كل جسم فهو مركب، وكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره. فكل مركب فهو محتاج إلى غيره.
As for His saying, glorified and exalted, 'Allah al-Samad', al-Samad is the master to whom one turns in needs. And that indicates that He is not a body, and that He is not specific to space and direction. As for the explanation of its indication against corporeality, it is from aspects: The first: Every body is composite, and every composite is in need of each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it. So every composite is in need of other than itself.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage begins the proof from the divine name 'al-Samad'. Razi first provides the lexical meaning: al-Samad is the master (sayyid) to whom all creatures turn for their needs (masmud ilayhi al-hawa'ij). This meaning itself implies that Allah is not a body and not confined to space or direction. Then he starts enumerating the specific proofs ('wujuh') that 'al-Samad' disproves corporeality. The first face (al-awwal) stated here is: every body is composite, every composite depends on its parts, each part is other than itself, so every composite depends on something other than itself. The implication (not fully stated here due to page break) is that Allah, being al-Samad, is independent and thus not a body. The text cuts off mid-proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-Samad' means only 'eternal' or 'self-sufficient' without realizing it also negates composition and dependence.
Correction: Razi shows that 'al-Samad' literally means the master to whom all needs are directed, which logically excludes any need for parts or external support, thus negating corporeality.

Key Takeaway
The name al-Samad, meaning the Master to whom all needs are directed, inherently entails that Allah is not a body, because bodies are composite and dependent on their parts.

Sixth Argument: Al-Khaliq and Al-Musawwir
Imam Razi proves God's incorporeality from His attributes of creating and fashioning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the divine attributes of Creator and Fashioner to refute corporealism.

Core Idea
If God were a body, His attributes would imply self-determination, which is impossible.

This lesson presents Imam Razi's sixth argument against the claim that God is a body. By analyzing the linguistic meanings of 'Khaliq' and 'Musawwir', Razi shows that corporealism leads to absurd circularity. The passage demonstrates the power of divine attributes as proofs for tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note its two-part structure
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice that the argument consists of two parts introduced by 'also' (أيضا).
The first part uses 'Khaliq', the second 'Musawwir'.
2
Understand the meaning of 'Khaliq' as 'Determiner'
Imam Razi explains that 'Khaliq' linguistically means 'Muqaddir' (Determiner). If God were a body, He would be finite and have a specific measure, but as Creator He determines all measures—so He would be determining Himself, an impossibility.
This shows that God cannot be a body because He is the source of all measures.
3
Recognize the parallel argument from 'Musawwir'
If God were a body, He would be finite and have a shape/form. But He describes Himself as the Fashioner of forms—so He would be fashioning Himself, another impossibility.
Both arguments converge on the same conclusion: God is transcendent above body and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة: قوله تعالى: ( هو الله الخالق البارئ المصور ) وجه الاستدلال به: إنا بينا في سائر كتبنا أن الخالق في اللغة هو المقدر. ولو كان تعالى جسماً لكان متناهيا، ولو كان متناهيا لكان مخصوصا بمقدار معين، ولما وصف نفسه بكونه خالقا، وجب أن يكون تعالى المقدر لجميع المقدرات بمقاديرها المخصوصة، وإذا كان هو مقدراً في ذاته بمقدار مخصوص لزم كونه مقدراً لنفسه، وذلك محال. وأيضا: لو كان جسماً، لكان متناهيا. وكل متناه، فإنه محيط به حد واحد أو حدود مختلفة. وكل ما كان كذلك، فهو مشكل. وكل مشكل فله صورة. فلو كان جسما لكان له صورة. ثم إنه تعالى وصف نفسه بكونه مصورا، فيلزم كونه مصورا لنفسه. وذلك محال. فيلزم أن يكون منزها عن الصورة والجسمية حتى لا يلزم هذا المحال.
The sixth proof: His saying, exalted is He: {He is Allah, the Creator, the Maker, the Fashioner}. The aspect of argumentation: we have explained in our other books that 'al-Khaliq' in language means 'the Determiner'. If He, exalted, were a body, He would be finite; if finite, He would be specified by a particular measure. And since He described Himself as being 'Creator', He must be the Determiner of all determined things with their particular measures. If He were Himself determined in His essence by a particular measure, then He would be a determiner of Himself, and that is impossible. Also: if He were a body, He would be finite. Every finite thing is encompassed by a single boundary or different boundaries. Everything that is like that has a shape. Every shape has a form. So if He were a body, He would have a form. Then He, exalted, described Himself as being 'Fashioner'. This would necessitate that He be a fashioner of Himself, which is impossible. Therefore, it necessitates that He be transcendent above form and corporeality, so that this impossible consequence does not follow.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument against corporealism. Imam Razi uses the verse (59:24) where God describes Himself as al-Khaliq (Creator), al-Bari' (Maker), and al-Musawwir (Fashioner). The argument has two parts, both following a similar logical structure: if God were a body, an absurd consequence would follow from His own attributes. First, from "Khaliq": He explains that "Khaliq" linguistically means "Muqaddir" (Determiner). If God were a body, He would be finite (mutanahi) and thus specified by a particular measure. But as Creator, He determines all measures. If He Himself were determined by a measure, He would be a determiner of Himself, which is impossible. Second, from "Musawwir": If God were a body, He would be finite, encompassed by boundaries, and thus have a shape (shakl). Having a shape implies having a form (sura). But He describes Himself as the Fashioner of forms, so He would be fashioning Himself, which is impossible. Therefore, God must be free from body and form.

Key Terms
الخالق
المقدر
المصور
متناه
حد
شكل
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being a determiner of measures does not preclude being determined, but the argument shows it leads to circularity.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that if God were a body with a specific measure, then as the Determiner of all measures, He would be the determiner of His own measure, which is absurd. Thus, He cannot be a body.

Checkpoint
What two attributes from the verse does Imam Razi use to argue against corporealism?
A) al-Khaliq and al-Bari'
B) al-Khaliq and al-Musawwir
C) al-Bari' and al-Musawwir
D) al-Khaliq and al-Qadir

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a body because that would make Him a determiner of Himself and a fashioner of Himself, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God in your mind, do you unconsciously picture a form or limit? How can this lesson help you purify your understanding of God from hidden anthropomorphism?
?
The argument shows that God cannot determine Himself or fashion Himself. But do you sometimes try to 'fashion' God to fit your own desires or expectations? What would it take to surrender that image and worship God as He is—beyond all forms?
?
If God is absolutely transcendent and not a body, what does that mean for your daily worship? How does it change the way you speak to Him, rely on Him, or envision His nearness?

The Verse 'al-Zahir wa al-Batin' Refutes Corporealism
Imam Razi argues that corporealism contradicts God's being both Apparent and Hidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the Qur'anic verse 'al-Zahir wa al-Batin' to refute corporealism through both a literal and an exegetical argument.

Core Idea
God's being both Apparent and Hidden refutes the notion that He is a body.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the seventh argument against corporealism, drawing from the verse (57:3). He demonstrates that if God were a body, He could not be simultaneously described as both 'Apparent' and 'Hidden' without contradiction. The argument unfolds in two clear steps, each exposing the incompatibility of corporealism with these divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and translation to grasp Imam Razi's flow.
Notice the two-part structure: first the literal objection, then the exegetical interpretation.
2
Understand the first argument: literal surface/depth
Imam Razi argues that a body has an apparent surface and a hidden interior, so the same thing cannot be both. God explicitly says He is both, so He cannot be a body.
This is a direct logical contradiction.
3
Understand the second argument: exegetical interpretation
The exegetes explain that 'Apparent' means evident through proofs, and 'Hidden' means inaccessible to senses and imagination. If God were a body, He would be perceivable, contradicting His hiddenness.
This argument relies on accepted interpretation.
4
Synthesize the argument
Combine both parts to see how the verse provides a powerful proof against corporealism, as it requires God to be both manifest and veiled in a way impossible for a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: قوله تعالى: ( هو الأول والآخر والظاهر والباطن ) وصف نفسه بكونه ظاهرا وباطنا. ولو كان جسما لكان ظاهره غير باطنه. فلم يكن الشيء الواحد موصوفا بأنه ظاهر وبأنه باطن، لأن على تقدير كونه جسما، يكون الظاهر منه سطحه، والباطن منه عمقه، فلم يكن الشيء الواحد ظاهرا وباطنا. وأيضا: فالمفسرون قالوا: إنه ظاهر بحسب الدلائل، باطن بحسب أنه لا يدركه الحس، ولا يصل إليه الخيال. ولو كان جسما لما أمكن وصفه بأنه لا يدركه الحس، ولا يصل إليه الخيال.
The seventh proof: His saying, exalted is He: {He is the First and the Last, the Apparent and the Hidden}. He described Himself as being Apparent and Hidden. If He were a body, His apparent part would be other than His hidden part. So the one thing would not be described as both apparent and hidden, because on the assumption that He is a body, the apparent part of Him would be its surface, and the hidden part would be its depth. So the one thing would not be both apparent and hidden. Also: the exegetes said: He is Apparent with respect to proofs, Hidden with respect to the fact that sense perception does not perceive Him nor imagination reach Him. If He were a body, it would not be possible to describe Him as not being perceived by sense or reached by imagination.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument against corporealism. It is based on the verse (57:3) where God describes Himself as al-Zahir (the Apparent) and al-Batin (the Hidden). The argument has two parts. First, if God were a body, He would have an external surface and an internal depth. The same entity would not be both apparent (surface) and hidden (depth) simultaneously; these would be different aspects. But God describes Himself as both without differentiation. Second, the exegetes interpret 'Apparent' as manifest through proofs and 'Hidden' as imperceptible to senses and imagination. If God were a body, He would be perceivable by senses and imaginable, contradicting His being Hidden. Therefore, corporealism is inconsistent with these attributes.

Key Terms
الظاهر
الباطن
سطح
عمق
حس
خيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God can be both apparent and hidden literally as a body, but the argument shows that requires two different parts.
Correction: A body's apparent part (surface) is distinct from its hidden part (depth), so the same thing cannot be both. The verse implies a unity of attribute that a body cannot possess.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument Imam Razi makes from the verse 'al-Zahir wa al-Batin'?
A) If God were a body, He would have surface and depth, so the same thing would not be both apparent and hidden.
B) The verse proves God is visible to the senses.
C) The exegetes have no interpretation.
D) The verse applies to prophets only.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'He is the First and the Last, the Apparent and the Hidden' provides a rational refutation of corporealism, as a body cannot be both apparent and hidden in the way God is.

How can you cultivate more certainty in the unseen while appreciating the signs?
?
The argument shows that if God were a body, He would be limited and divided. Do you ever instinctively think of God in terms of space or location, even though you know He is not physical? Second Reason: If God Were a Body, His Attributes Would Be Contingent
Imam Razi's second reason: similarity argument against divine corporeality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's second argument for why God cannot be a body, based on the contingency of divine attributes.

Core Idea
If God were like bodies, His attributes would require a cause, making Him dependent.

Imam Razi presents his second reason against corporealism. He argues that if God were similar to bodies, then His attributes would share the same contingent status as bodily accidents. This would mean that God's life, knowledge, and power are not necessary but brought about by another, which is impossible. Let's follow his logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of similarity
Recognize that if two things are similar, they share the same properties (concomitants).
This is a logical principle.
2
Apply to bodies' lack of attributes
Bodies can be devoid of knowledge, power, and life; so if God were a body, He too could lack them.
3
Realize the implication for God's actual attributes
If God could lack these attributes, then His actual possession of them would be contingent, needing a cause.
4
Conclude that God cannot be a body
A dependent being cannot be God; therefore God is not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن المثلين يحب استواؤهما في جميع اللوازم . وكما صح على سائر الأجسام خلوها عن صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة ، وجب أن يصح على ذاته المخلو عن هذه الصفات، وحينئذ يكون اتصاف ذاته بحياته وعلمه وقدرته ، من الجائزات . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كون تلك الذات موصوفاً بالحياة والعلم والقدرة ، إلا بإيجاد موجد ، وتخصيص مخصص. وذلك يقتضى احتياجه إلى الإله . وحينئذ كل ما كان جسماً كان محتاجاً إلى الإله . وهذا يقتضى أن الإله يمتنع أن يكون جسماً
The second: Two similar things require equality in all concomitants. And just as it is valid for all bodies to be devoid of the attributes of knowledge, power, and life, it must be valid for His essence to be devoid of these attributes; then His essence's being described with His life, knowledge, and power would be among contingent matters. And if the matter is such, it is impossible for that essence to be described with life, knowledge, and power, except by a causing of a cause and a specification of a specifier. That necessitates its need for a god. And then everything that is a body is in need of a god. And this necessitates that God cannot be a body.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the second reason. It argues that if God were a body, then He would be similar to bodies, and similar things share all concomitants. Bodies can be devoid of knowledge, power, and life (they are accidental). So God could also be devoid of these attributes. In that case, His actual possession of life, knowledge, and power would be contingent, requiring a cause to bring them about. That would make Him need a god, which is absurd. Hence God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
مثلان
لازم
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument denies that God has attributes.
Correction: It actually shows that if God were a body, His attributes would be contingent, not that they are absent.

Checkpoint

A) He would have no attributes.
B) His attributes would be necessary.
C) His attributes would be contingent and need a cause.
D) He would still be God.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's second reason shows that if God were a body, His attributes would be contingent, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refutation of the Division of Knowledge among Parts
Razi refutes the claim that knowledge can be divided among parts of a composite being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing knowledge into parts among a composite being is logically impossible according to Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Dividing knowledge leads to either multiple knowledges or no knowledge at all.

Imam Razi addresses an objection: why cannot knowledge be divided so that each part of a composite being contains a portion? In this passage, he demonstrates the impossibility through a simple but powerful logical dichotomy. Let's trace his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the initial question: why can't knowledge be divided and allocated to parts?
The objection assumes knowledge can be split into parts.
2
Identify Razi's two-case analysis
Notice that Razi splits the problem into two possibilities for each part of knowledge: it is either knowledge itself or not knowledge. Trace the logical consequences for both cases.
This is a classic dichotomy argument.
3
Understand the first case: each part is knowledge
If each part is knowledge, then each part has its own independent knowledge, not a share of one knowledge. This contradicts the original claim that there is one knowledge for the whole.
4
Understand the second case: each part is not knowledge
If each part is not knowledge, then no part has knowledge. Since the whole is composed of these non-knowledge parts, the whole cannot have knowledge or power.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, both possibilities lead to absurdity, proving that the division of knowledge among parts is impossible.
This refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني وهو قوله لم لا يجوز أن يقال العلم ينقسم فقام بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء جزء واحد من ذلك فنقول هذا محال لأن كل واحد من أجزاء العلم إما أن يكون علما وإما أن لا يكون علما فإن كان الأول كان القائم بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء علما على حدة وذلك غير هذا السؤال وإن كان الثاني لم يكن شيء من تلك الأجزاء موصوفا بالعلم والمجموع ليس إلا تلك الأموا فوجب أن لا يكون المجموع موصوفا بالعلم والقدرة
And as for the second question, which is his saying: Why may it not be said that knowledge divides, and a single part of it subsists in each one of those parts? So we say: This is impossible. Because each one of the parts of knowledge is either knowledge or not knowledge. If it is the first, then that which subsists in each one of those parts is knowledge individually, and that is other than this question. And if it is the second, then none of those parts is described with knowledge. And the aggregate is nothing but those [things], so it is necessary that the aggregate not be described with knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage answers an objection: Why can't knowledge be divided so that each part of a composite being gets a portion? Razi argues this is impossible. The division of knowledge into parts: each part either is knowledge or is not. If it is knowledge, then each part has its own distinct knowledge, which is not the original claim (that the whole has one knowledge). If it is not knowledge, then no part has knowledge, so the whole (composed of non-knowledge parts) cannot have knowledge or power. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
العلم ينقسم
أجزاء العلم
القائم
المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dividing knowledge into parts allows a composite being to have one knowledge spread over its parts.
Correction: Razi shows that each part either is full knowledge (multiple knowledges) or is not knowledge (no knowledge), so division cannot yield a single knowledge for the whole.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, if each part of knowledge is not knowledge, what follows?
A) The whole has one knowledge
B) The whole has multiple knowledges
C) No part has knowledge, and the whole cannot have knowledge or power
D) Each part has a portion of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that knowledge cannot be divided into parts that collectively form one knowledge, because each part either is or is not knowledge, leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your knowledge of Allah is divided into separate compartments—what you believe in theory and what you practice? How does Razi's argument that knowledge is indivisible challenge you to unify your faith and actions? What specific step will you take today to close that gap?

If knowledge cannot be fragmented, then your understanding of Tawhid cannot be isolated to prayer alone. In which area of your life—work, family, or private thoughts—are you trying to keep your knowledge of Allah separate? What would it mean to let that knowledge permeate every decision, no matter how small?
?
Razi shows that if any part lacks knowledge, the whole lacks it. Think of your heart: are there 'parts' of your life where you have deliberately kept the light of knowledge out—habits, relationships, or fears? How does that darkness affect the whole of your faith? Will you invite knowledge into that area today?

Proof of Premise 1: God Knows Physical Things
Razi presents the proof for the first premise that God knows physical things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first premise of the objection is supported by both consensus and a rational argument.

Core Idea
God must know physical things because He created them.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the first premise of the objection: that God must be knowledgeable of physical things. He offers two lines of support: the consensus of Muslims and a rational argument based on God's role as Creator. This premise will later be refuted in the answer, but it is important to understand its structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that it begins with 'As for the first premise' and concludes with 'it is established.'
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage presents two separate proofs: first, the consensus (ijma') of Muslims; second, a rational argument from creation. Locate each.
The rational argument has three steps: (1) bodies need a creator, (2) that creator is God, (3) a creator knows its creation.
3
Understand the role of this premise
This premise is part of an objection that Razi will later refute. It is not his final position, but a claim he is testing. Keep this context in mind.
The lesson does not endorse the premise as ultimate truth.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasped the two bases for the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة الأولى: وهي أن إله العالم يجب أن يكون عالما بهذه الجسمانيات، فقد اتفق المسلمون عليها. وأيضا: فهذه الأجسام الموصوفة بهذه المقادير، لا بد لها من خالق. وذلك الخالق هو الله تعالى. وخالق الشيء لا بد وأن يكون عالما به. فثبت: أن خالق العالم عالم بهذه الجسمانيات.

So it is established that the Creator of the world is knowledgeable of these physical things.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the first premise of the objection. The author begins by noting that Muslims unanimously agree (ijma') that God knows physical things. Then he offers a rational argument: physical bodies with specific quantities need a creator; that creator is God; and the creator of something must know that thing. Therefore, the Creator (God) knows physical things. This proof is meant to support the objection, but it will later be refuted in the answer to the third objection.

Proof That God's Knowledge Contains Forms of Physical Things
Proof that the knower of physical things must have their images in his essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God's knowledge of physical things requires their forms to exist in His essence before creation.

Core Idea
To know a thing, its image must exist in the knower's essence.

This lesson unpacks a crucial premise in a theological argument about God's knowledge. The passage presents a proof that the Creator must have images of physical things in His essence before creating them. We will follow the logical steps that lead from the necessity of pre-creation knowledge to the conclusion that those known things exist in God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Try to grasp the flow of reasoning.
Note the logical connectors: يجب أن, وإلا, وإذا, ولما.
2
Identify the proposition and proof structure
The passage states the second premise (المقدمة الثانية) and then gives its proof (الدليل). Identify the claim and the sequence of conditional statements.
3
Analyze the key condition about distinction
The proof hinges on the idea that knowing a thing requires distinguishing it. Ask yourself: Why can't pure non-existence be distinguished?
This is the critical move to show the known thing must have some existence.
4
Trace the conclusion
Follow how the argument concludes that since the known thing does not exist externally, its existence must be in God's knowledge. This establishes the presence of forms in the divine essence.
The argument is dialectical – it is used to set up a problem, not as the author's own belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية: وهي في بيان أن العالم بهذه الجسمانيات، يجب أن يحصل في ذاته صور الجسمانيات. فالدليل عليه: أن خالقها يجب أن يكون عالما بها قبل وجودها، وإلا لم يصح منه خلقها وإيجادها. والعالم بالشيء يجب أن يتميز ذلك في علمه عن سائر المعلومات، وإلا لم يكن عالما به. وإذا تميز ذلك المعلوم عن غيره، فذلك المعلوم ليس عدما محضا، لأن العدم المحض لا يحصل فيه الامتياز. وذلك المعلوم يجب أن يكون أمرا موجودا. وهو غير موجود في الخارج - لأن الكلام فيه [؟] قبل وجودها - ولما لم يكن وجوده في الخارج، وجب أن يكون وجوده في علم صانع العالم.
As for the second premise — which is in explanation that the one knowledgeable of these physical things must have forms of physical things occur in his essence. The proof for it: that their Creator must be knowledgeable of them before their existence, otherwise His creating and bringing them into existence would not be valid. And the knower of a thing must distinguish that thing in his knowledge from other known things, otherwise he would not be knowledgeable of it. And when that known thing is distinguished from others, that known thing is not pure non-existence, because pure non-existence does not admit of distinction. And that known thing must be an existent thing. But it does not exist externally — because the discussion about it [？] before its existence — and since its existence is not external, its existence must be in the knowledge of the Maker of the world.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage proves the second premise: the knower of physical things must have images (suwar) of them in his essence. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) God must know physical things before they exist, otherwise He could not create them. (2) Knowledge of a thing requires distinguishing it from other known things. (3) That distinction cannot occur in pure non-existence; so the known thing must have some kind of existence. (4) Since it does not exist externally before creation, it must exist in God's knowledge. This establishes that forms of physical things exist in God's essence, which the objection uses to argue that God is a body.

عدم محض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is an assertion that God has a physical form or body.
Correction: This is a hypothetical premise used in a dialectical argument; it does not represent the author's final view. The argument is presented to be examined, not necessarily accepted.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is physical
B) Because pure non-existence cannot be distinguished
C) Because God created it
D) Because the intellect demands it

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that God's pre-creation knowledge of physical things requires their forms to have existence in His knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Karramiyya Argument and Ash'ari Refutation on Direction
Imam Razi presents the Karramiyya claim and Ash'ari refutation on God and direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya argument for God's direction based on visibility and the Ash'ari methodological critique of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen.

Core Idea
The seen-to-unseen analogy is not automatically valid in theology.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a classic theological debate: the Karramiyya claim that since God is seen (in the afterlife), He must occupy a direction. The Ash'ari school refutes this by questioning the premise and the analogical inference from the physical world to God. This lesson examines their methodology and the crucial distinction between shahid (witnessed) and ghā'ib (unseen).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then a refutation, then an analysis of the premise.
The Arabic text cuts off mid-sentence; the argument is incomplete but the key points are present.
2
Identify the Karramiyya claim
The Karramiyya say: 'but He is seen, so necessarily He is in a direction.' This is their core argument. Note that 'seen' refers to the vision of God in the afterlife.
3
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
The Ash'ari respond by disputing the premise that everything seen is specific to direction. They accept it applies in the witnessed world but ask why it must apply to the unseen God. Then they analyze whether the premise is axiomatic or discursive.
This highlights a key principle in kalam: the limits of analogical reasoning from creation to God.
4
Reflect on the shahid-ghā'ib distinction
Ask yourself why the Ash'ari emphasize the difference between the witnessed and the unseen. This distinction is fundamental in Islamic theology to avoid anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكرامية قالوا : لكنه مرتى ، فوجب أن يكون في الجهة . وأصحابنا .. رحمهم الله - نازعوا في هذه المقدمة. وقالوا : لا نسلم أن كل مرتى فإنه مختص بالجهة . بل لا نزاع ) فى ) (٤٤) أن الأمر في الشاهد كذلك . لكن. لم قلتم : إن ( ما كان ) (٤٠) في الشاهد كذلك ، وجب أن يكون في الغائب كذلك ؟ وتقريره : إن هذه المقدمة إما أن تكون مقدمة بديهية أو استدلالية . فإن كانت بديهية لم يكن في إثبات كونه تعالى مختصاً بالجهة حاجة إلى هذا الدليل . ذلك لأنه ثبت في الشاهد أن كل قائم بالنفس
And the Karramiyya said: but He is seen (visible?), so necessarily He is in a direction. And our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) disputed this premise. And they said: we do not accept that everything seen is specific to direction. Rather, there is no dispute (in) (44) that the matter in the witnessed (shahid) is such. But why do you say: that which is such in the witnessed must also be such in the unseen (gha'ib)? And its exposition: that this premise is either an axiomatic (badihiyya) premise or a discursive (istidlaliyya) one. If it is axiomatic, then there is no need for this proof in establishing that the Exalted is specific to direction. That is because it is established in the witnessed that every self-subsistent...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The Karramiyya sect argues that since God is seen (in the afterlife), He must occupy a direction (jiha). The Ash'ari school (the author's school) refutes this by questioning the premise. They argue that it is not accepted that everything seen is necessarily located in a direction. Then they raise a methodological point: even if this holds in the witnessed world (shahid), why must it apply to the unseen (gha'ib)? The author then analyzes the premise: if it is axiomatic, then the argument would be unnecessary; if it is discursive, it requires proof. The text cuts off, but the argument is clearly a standard Ash'ari critique of analogical reasoning from the visible to the divine.

Key Terms
الكرامية (Karramiyya)
مرتى (visible?)
الجهة (direction)
الشاهد (witnessed)
الغائب (unseen)
مقدمة بديهية (axiomatic premise)
مقدمة استدلالية (discursive premise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That because God is seen (in the afterlife), He must be physically located in a direction, like created objects.
Correction: The Ash'ari refute this by showing that the analogical inference from the witnessed to the unseen is not automatic. The premise that 'everything seen is in a direction' is not necessarily true for God, as the unseen realm may differ fundamentally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Karramiyya's argument for God being in a direction?
A) God is omnipresent
B) God is seen, therefore He is in a direction
C) God is in heaven
D) God is not seen

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's analogical argument from visibility to direction is refuted by challenging the premise and the validity of transferring rules from the witnessed to the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you unconsciously assign Him a direction or location? How does this lesson reveal the subtle anthropomorphism in your heart, and what will you do to purify your belief about Allah's transcendence?
?
Are you quick to apply earthly logic to divine matters without examining the premises? Give a specific situation in your life where you have done this, and commit to being more cautious in your reasoning.
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly obvious analogy (seen implies direction) can be flawed. How does this affect your trust in your own reasoning about Allah's attributes? Will you now humbly acknowledge the limits of human intellect in matters of tawheed, and seek knowledge from the Qur'an and Sunnah instead?
