Chapter Four on the word 'Al-Samad'. Allah Most High said: 'Allah, Al-Samad.' Some have mentioned in the interpretation of 'Al-Samad' that it is the body which has no cavity. And from that is the saying of one who says for the stopper of the bottle: 'al-ʿimād'. And a thing 'muṣammad' meaning solid, having no softness. Ibn Qutaybah said: according to this interpretation, the dāl is substituted for the tā'. And some said: 'Al-Samad' is the smooth thing, from the stone that does not accept dust, neither does anything enter it nor anything come out of it. A group of the ignorant anthropomorphists used this verse to prove that He Most High is a body. This is false. For we have shown that His being 'Aḥad' (One) contradicts His being a body. So the preamble of this verse indicates that it is not possible that what is meant by 'Al-Samad' is this meaning. And because 'Al-Samad' according to this interpretation is an attribute of coarse bodies, and Allah is exalted above that. The answer to it is from two aspects: First: that 'Al-Samad' is a verbal noun in the sense of a passive participle, from 'ṣamada ilayhi' meaning he intended. And the meaning is that He is the one to whom one turns for needs. The poet said: 'Does not the announcer of death call the bewildered of Banī Asad, by ʿAmr ibn Masʿūd and the master, al-Ṣamad?' And another said: 'I overcame him with my sword, then said to him: take it, Ḥudhayf, for you are the master, al-Ṣamad.' And what indicates the correctness of this aspect is what Ibn ʿAbbās (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated: when this verse was revealed, they said: what is 'Al-Ṣamad'? He (peace be upon him) said: 'The master to whom one turns in needs.' Abū al-Layth said: 'ṣamadtu ṣamada hādhā al-amr' meaning I intended its direction. The second aspect in the answer: We concede that 'Al-Ṣamad' in the original language is the solid thing into which nothing else enters. However we say: we have demonstrated that it is not possible to establish this meaning with respect to Allah Most High, so it is necessary to carry this word to its metaphorical sense. For the body that is such is free from division, separation, and being affected by others. And He - glorified and exalted - is necessary of existence by Himself. And that requires that He Most High is not subject to increase or decrease. So what is meant by 'Al-Ṣamad' with respect to His (?) Most High is this meaning. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the anthropomorphist (mushabbiha) interpretation of the divine name 'Al-Samad' from Surah Al-Ikhlas. Razi begins by citing various lexical interpretations of the word: some say it means a solid body with no cavity, like a stopper; others say it means smooth stone that nothing enters or leaves. He then states that a group of ignorant anthropomorphists used this verse to argue that God is a body. Razi dismisses this as false, giving two reasons: first, the preceding phrase 'Allah, Al-Ahad' (God, the One) negates composition, and if God is One, He cannot be a body. Second, the interpretation of 'Samad' as solid applies to coarse bodies, and God is exalted above that. Then Razi provides two detailed answers to the anthropomorphists' claim. The first answer argues that 'Samad' is actually a verbal noun meaning 'the one to whom one turns for needs' (al-maṣmūd ilayhi fī al-ḥawā'ij). He supports this with two lines of poetry and a hadith from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) defined 'Al-Samad' as 'the master to whom one turns in needs'. The second answer concedes that the lexical meaning might be 'solid and impenetrable', but argues that even then it cannot be applied literally to God. Instead, it must be taken metaphorically, because a body that is solid and impenetrable is free from division and being affected—attributes that suit God's necessary existence and immutability. Thus, the intended meaning is that God is not subject to increase, decrease, or change. Razi concludes with 'Wa billāhi al-tawfīq' (And with Allah is success).

Key Terms
الصمد
المشبهة
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if 'Samad' means solid, then God must be a solid body.
Correction: Razi shows that the qualities of a solid body (impenetrability, lack of division) metaphorically apply to God's transcendence, not to a physical form.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue is the metaphorical meaning of 'Al-Samad' when applied to God?
A) That God is a solid body
B) That God is independent and unchanging
C) That God is a cavity-free body
D) That God is the Master to whom one turns

Key Takeaway
Razi transforms the literal meaning of 'Al-Samad' as 'solid' into a metaphor for God's necessary existence and immunity to change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as 'Al-Samad'—the Self-Sufficient—do you truly rely on Him alone, or do you still trust in worldly means as if they have independent power? How does Razi's stripping away of literal solidity expose the subtle idolatry in your heart?
?
Razi argues that God is not subject to increase or decrease. Are there areas in your life where you imagine God's love or favor can be increased by your actions? Does your daily worship reflect a belief that God is complete without you, or do you act as if He needs your obedience?
?
If you had to defend God's transcendence without using metaphors, would you feel lost? How often do you catch yourself picturing God in human terms, and what would it take for you to embrace a God beyond all images—even if it feels uncomfortable?

First Interpretation: Allah's Signs Come
Razi presents the first interpretation: the verse refers to the coming of Allah's signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretive approach (ta'wil) offered by Imam Razi to resolve the apparent anthropomorphism in the verse, and the rhetorical principle of tafkhiim that justifies it.

Core Idea
When the verse says 'Allah comes,' it means His signs come, magnifying their importance.

When the Qur'anic language seems to ascribe physical movement to Allah, Muslim theologians have developed careful interpretive methods to protect divine transcendence. Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, in his work Asas al-Taqdis, presents several such interpretations for a particular verse. In this passage, he offers the first: the 'coming' is actually the coming of Allah's signs (ayat), a rhetorical device of magnification (tafkhiim). This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi frames this as the 'first aspect' of interpretation.
2
Identify the rhetorical analogy
Razi compares the verse to saying 'the king has come' when his army arrives. Reflect on how this analogy supports the interpretation.
The key term here is 'tafkhiim' (magnification).
3
Examine the scriptural support
Razi cites the preceding verse (al-Baqarah 209) which mentions 'clear proofs' in a context of warning. See how this supports the interpretation that 'coming' refers to signs.
The verse is about rebuke and threat, reinforcing the idea of evidential coming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثاني وهو في بيان التأويلات المذكورة في هذه الآية: فنقول: فيه وجوه: الأول : المراد : هل ينظرون إلا أن تأتيهم آيات الله. فجعل مجيء آيات الله مجيئا له . على التفخيم لشأن الآيات . كما يقال : جاء الملك . إذا جاء جيش عظيم من جهته ، والذي يدل على صحة هذا التأويل : أنه تعالى قال في الآية المتقدمة : فإن زللتم من بعد ما جاءتكم البينات ، فاعلموا : أن الله عزيز حكيم فذكر ذلك في معرض الزجر والتهديد . ثم إنه تعالى أكد [؟]
As for the second type, which is explanation of the interpretations mentioned concerning this verse, we say: there are aspects to it: The first: the meaning is 'Do they wait for anything except that the signs of Allah should come to them?' So He made the coming of Allah's signs a coming of Himself, for the magnification of the status of the signs. Just as it is said: 'The king has come' when a great army from his direction comes. And that which indicates the validity of this interpretation is that He, exalted, said in the preceding verse: 'But if you slip after the clear proofs have come to you, then know that Allah is Mighty, Wise.' He mentioned that in the context of rebuke and threat. Then He, exalted, emphasized [؟]
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the second type of discussion: providing interpretive solutions (ta'wīl) for the verse that seems to attribute physical movement to Allah. The author lists multiple 'aspects' (wujuh) of interpretation; here he gives the first one.

The interpretation: the phrase 'that Allah should come to them' actually means 'that the signs (āyāt) of Allah should come to them.' The attribution of the coming to Allah is a rhetorical device for exaltation (tafkhīm) of the signs, similar to saying 'the king has come' when a great army from his direction arrives. The author then supports this interpretation by citing a previous verse (al-Baqarah 209) where Allah mentions the coming of clear proofs (bayyināt) in a context of warning. This parallelism suggests that the coming in the disputed verse refers to evidence, not a physical arrival. The sentence ends abruptly with 'then He emphasized [؟]', likely introducing another supporting point that is cut off.

المجيء

B) It means the signs of Allah come.
C) It is a metaphor for Allah's wrath.
D) It means angels come.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first interpretation resolves the apparent anthropomorphism by understanding 'Allah's coming' as the coming of His signs, a rhetorical magnification supported by the context of warning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The previous verse (al-Baqarah 209) uses the coming of clear proofs as a warning. How does recognizing the context of threat and mercy change the way you read the verse? Will you commit to studying the context of each verse before forming an understanding?

The Two Meanings of 'Amr Allah'
Al-Razi refutes the objection that 'amr Allah' is eternal by distinguishing two meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi resolves the paradox of the eternal command 'coming' by showing that 'amr' can refer to a created action.

Core Idea
The word 'amr' can mean a created action, not only the eternal attribute.

This passage presents an objection against the Ash'ari position that Allah's command (amr) is an eternal attribute. The objector argues that if it is eternal, then it cannot 'come' as the Quran describes. Al-Razi's answer reveals a crucial linguistic distinction that preserves both the eternity of the attribute and the literal wording of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection: if Allah's command (amr) is an eternal attribute, then its 'coming' (ityān) is impossible because attributes do not move.
2
Identify al-Razi's response
He says 'amr' in Arabic has two meanings: action (fi‘l) and way/affair (ṭarīq). This distinction allows him to interpret the verse differently.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the two Quranic verses he cites: 54:50 and 11:97. In both, 'amr' clearly refers to an action or event, not an eternal attribute.
The first verse describes creation as swift; the second refers to Pharaoh's affair.
4
Apply to the verse under discussion
In the verse about the coming of Allah's command, 'amr' means the awesome events of the Day of Judgment—a created action, not the eternal attribute.
5
Grasp the doctrinal implication
This interpretation defends the literal text while upholding divine transcendence: the eternal attribute remains unchanged, and what 'comes' is a temporal act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : أمر الله - عندكم - : صفة قديمة. فالإتيان عليها : محال . قلنا الأمر فى اللغة له معنيان : أحدهما : الفعل . والثاني : الطريق . قال تعالى : «وما أمرنا إلا واحدة كلمح بالبصر» ، وقال : « وما أمر فرعون برشيد » ، فيحمل الأمر فى هذه الآية على الفعل وهو ما يليق بتلك المواقف من الأهول، وإظهار الآيات المهيبة ، وهذا هو التأويل الأول الذي ذكرناه .
For if it is said: 'The command of Allah – according to you – is an eternal attribute. So the coming of it is impossible.' We say: 'Amr' in the language has two meanings: one is action, and the second is way/affair. He, exalted, said: 'And Our command is but one, like a glance of the eye' (Quran 54:50), and He said: 'And the command of Pharaoh was not right' (Quran 11:97). So the word 'amr' in this verse is interpreted as action, which is what befits those terrifying situations and the manifestation of majestic signs. And this is the first interpretation we mentioned.'
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit within al-Razi's tafsir. The objection is: if 'amr Allah' (command of Allah) is, according to the Ash'ari doctrine, an eternal attribute, then the concept of 'coming' (itiya) of that attribute is impossible because attributes do not move or arrive. Al-Razi responds by noting that the Arabic word 'amr' has two meanings: (1) action or event (fi'l), and (2) way or affair (tariq). He cites two Quranic verses: 'Our command is but one, like a glance of the eye' where 'amr' clearly refers to a swift act of creation, and 'The command of Pharaoh was not right' where 'amr' means his affair or way. Therefore, in the verse under discussion, 'amr' can be interpreted as action (fi'l) – specifically the awesome events of the Day of Judgment – rather than the eternal attribute. This defense allows the literal wording of 'coming of the command' to be acceptable without implying temporal arrival of the divine essence or attribute.

Key Terms
أمر الله
صفة قديمة
الإتيان
الفعل
الطريق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'amr Allah' always refers to the eternal attribute of command, and any literal description of it 'coming' implies a change in Allah's essence.
Correction: Al-Razi demonstrates that 'amr' can also mean a created action, so the 'coming' can refer to temporal events without affecting the eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi respond to the objection that the 'amr' of Allah is an eternal attribute whose coming is impossible?
A) He denies that 'amr' is an attribute
B) He argues that 'amr' can mean a created action, not the eternal attribute
C) He says the verse is metaphorical and should not be taken literally
D) He claims the objection is based on a misunderstanding of the Arabic grammar

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's linguistic distinction shows that the word 'amr' can refer to a created action, thus the 'coming' of Allah's command is unproblematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that describes Allah's 'coming' or 'command', do you immediately assume it implies change in God? How does this lesson challenge you to pause and examine the linguistic context before reaching a conclusion that might contradict Allah's transcendence?
?
Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah's attributes that you have never questioned, simply because you assumed a word always means the same thing? What would it cost you to re-examine those assumptions in light of the Quran's own language and usage?
?
If you were to apply Al-Razi's method of distinguishing meanings to other difficult verses, what would that require of you in terms of humility and intellectual effort? How ready are you to let the Quran shape your theology rather than imposing your preconceived notions on it?

Razi's Scriptural Proof for God's Face
Razi presents two proofs that God's face is not identical to His essence, then cites nine Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rational and scriptural argument that the 'face of God' mentioned in the Quran is a distinct attribute, not a reference to God Himself.

Core Idea
The 'face of God' is an attribute distinct from His essence, not a reference to God Himself.

Razi begins his treatment of the divine attribute 'face' by addressing a crucial confusion: when the Quran speaks of God's face, does it refer to God Himself? He presents two clear proofs that it does not, and then marshals nine Quranic verses that consistently use 'face' as an attribute. This lesson walks through those proofs and the scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the argument. Then read the English translation carefully. Notice how Razi structures his proof: first the two rational arguments, then the Quranic verses.
The passage is cut off at the ninth verse, but the argument is complete enough.
2
Focus on the two proofs
Razi gives two proofs that 'the Face of the Lord' is not the Lord Himself. Find them: the first is based on the impossibility of a thing being attributed to itself. The second is a grammatical argument about the case ending of 'Possessor of Majesty'.
The second proof relies on Arabic grammar: the attribute of a genitive noun must be genitive.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Razi lists nine verses that use 'face' (وجه) in reference to God. The first verse is explained with the two proofs; the remaining eight are cited without further analysis. Consider why these verses collectively support the existence of a divine attribute called 'face'.
This list shows that the Quran consistently differentiates between God and His face.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث عشر
في
الوجه:
احتجوا على إثبات الله تعالى بالآيات والأخبار:
أما الآيات فكثيرة:
أحدها: قوله تعالى: (كل من عليها فان. ويبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام،) قالوا: وامتنع أن يكون وجه الرب، هو الرب، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنه تعالى أضاف الوجه إلى نفسه. وإضافة الشيء إلى نفسه: ممتنعة. والثاني: لو كان ذو الجلال صفة للرب، لوجب أن يقال ذي الجلال، لأن صفة المجرور مجرورة. وثانيتها: قوله تعالى: كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه. وثالثتها: قوله تعالى: « واصبر نفسك مع الذين يدعون ربهم بالغداة والعشي، يريدون وجهه، ورابعها: قوله تعالى : ولا تطرد الذين يدعون ربهم بالغداة والعشي، يريدون وجهه، وخامستها : قوله تعالى : ( ولله المشرق والمغرب. فأينما تولوا ، فثم وجه الله، وسادستها : قوله تعالى ( في سورة الروم) و يريدون وجه الله وسابعها : قوله تعالى: «وما آتيتم من زكاة تريدون وجه الله، وثامنتها : قوله تعالى : ( إنما نطعمكم لوجه الله، . وتاسعتها : قوله تعالى : -
The thirteenth chapter: On the aspect. They argued for affirming God, the Exalted, by the verses and the reports. As for the verses, they are many: First: His statement, the Exalted: 'Everyone upon it is perishing. And there remains the Face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor.' They said: It is impossible that the Face of the Lord is the Lord, and two proofs indicate that: First: He, the Exalted, attributed the face to Himself. And attributing a thing to itself is impossible. Second: If 'Possessor of Majesty' were an attribute of the Lord, it would have to be said 'Dhi al-Jalal' (in the genitive), because the attribute of the genitive is genitive. And the second: His statement, the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' And the third: His statement, the Exalted: 'And keep yourself patient with those who call upon their Lord morning and evening, seeking His Face.' And the fourth: His statement, the Exalted: 'And do not drive away those who call upon their Lord morning and evening, seeking His Face.' And the fifth: His statement, the Exalted: 'And to Allah belongs the east and the west. So wherever you turn, there is the Face of Allah.' And the sixth: His statement, the Exalted (in Surat ar-Rum): 'And they desire the Face of Allah.' And the seventh: His statement, the Exalted: 'And whatever you give of zakah desiring the Face of Allah.' And the eighth: His statement, the Exalted: 'We only feed you for the Face of Allah.' And the ninth: His statement, the Exalted: -
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a scriptural proof for affirming God's 'face' (الوجه) as a divine attribute. Razi first presents two arguments to show that the 'face of the Lord' is not identical to the Lord Himself: 1) The face is attributed to God, and a thing cannot be attributed to itself (إضافة الشيء إلى نفسه ممتنعة). 2) Grammatically, if 'Possessor of Majesty' (ذو الجلال) were a description of the Lord, it would have to be in the genitive case (ذي الجلال) to match the preceding genitive phrase, but it appears in the nominative (ذو), indicating it is a description of 'face' not of 'Lord'. Then Razi lists nine Quranic verses (the first is explained, the rest merely cited) that use the word 'face' (وجه) in reference to God. The list is cut off at the ninth verse.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that when the Quran says 'Face of God', it means God Himself, either as a synonym or as a metaphorical expression for His essence.
Correction: Razi shows that the phrase 'Face of God' refers to a distinct attribute, not to God's essence, based on two proofs: the impossibility of self-attribution and the grammatical case ending.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses both rational and scriptural evidence to affirm that God's face is a distinct attribute, not a reference to His essence.

The Rational Proof that the Face of Allah is an Attribute
Rational argument from the first Quranic verse on the Face.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses linguistic and logical reasoning from the Quranic verse to demonstrate that the Face of Allah is a distinct attribute, not identical to the essence.

Core Idea
Adding a thing to itself is impossible — so the Face is not the Essence.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a rational argument embedded within the scriptural discussion. He quotes the verse from Surat ar-Rahman and then breaks down its grammatical and logical implications to prove that the Face of Allah is a created attribute, not the divine essence itself. This approach shows how kalam integrates text with reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the two arguments
Read the opening verse and the two rational proofs that follow. Notice how the argument is structured as a direct response to the verse.
The argument is presented by the proponents of affirming the Face.
2
Understand the first argument
The first proof: Allah adds the Face to Himself. Adding a thing to itself is impossible, so the Face must be other than the essence.
3
Examine the second argument
The second proof: grammatical analysis of 'Dhu al-Jalal'. If it described 'Lord' (genitive), it should be 'Dhi al-Jalal'. Its nominative case shows it describes 'Face'.
This reinforces that the Face is a distinct entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث عشر
فى الوجه
احتجوا على إثباته الله تعالى بالآيات والأخبار
أما الآيات فكثيرة
أحدها: قوله تعالى: "كل من عليها فان ويبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام"
قالوا: وامتنع أن يكون وجه الرب هو الرب، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنه تعالى أضاف الوجه إلى نفسه، وإضافة الشيء إلى نفسه ممتنعة. والثاني: لو كان ذو الجلال صفة للرب، لوجب أن يقال ذي الجلال، لأن صفة المجرور مجرورة.
وثانيتها: قوله تعالى: "كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه"
وثالثتها: قوله تعالى: "واصبر نفسك مع الذين يدعون ربهم بالغداة والعشي يريدون وجهه"
ورابعتها: قوله تعالى: "ولا تطرد الذين يدعون ربهم بالغداة والعشي يريدون وجهه"
وخامستها: قوله تعالى: "ولله المشرق والمغرب فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله"
وسادستها: قوله تعالى: "و يريدون وجه الله" (في سورة الروم)
وسابعتها: قوله تعالى: "وما آتيتم من زكاة تريدون وجه الله"
وثامنتها: قوله تعالى: "إنما نطعمكم لوجه الله"
وتاسعتها: قوله تعالى: "إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى"
The Thirteenth Chapter on the Face. They argued for affirming it (the Face) of Allah the Exalted by verses and hadiths. As for the verses, they are many. First: His saying the Exalted: 'Everyone upon it is perishing, and there remains the Face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor.' They said: It is impossible that the Face of the Lord be the Lord, and evidence for this is two aspects: First: He the Exalted added the Face to Himself, and adding a thing to itself is impossible. Second: If 'Possessor of Majesty' were an attribute of the Lord, it would be necessary to say 'Dhi al-Jalal' (in the genitive), because the attribute of the genitive is genitive. And second: His saying the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' And third: His saying the Exalted: 'And keep yourself patient with those who call upon their Lord morning and evening, desiring His Face.' And fourth: His saying the Exalted: 'And do not repel those who call upon their Lord morning and evening, desiring His Face.' And fifth: His saying the Exalted: 'And to Allah belongs the east and the west; so wherever you turn, there is the Face of Allah.' And sixth: His saying the Exalted: 'And they desire the Face of Allah' (in Surat ar-Rum). And seventh: His saying the Exalted: 'And whatever you give of zakah, desiring the Face of Allah.' And eighth: His saying the Exalted: 'We only feed you for the Face of Allah.' And ninth: His saying the Exalted: 'Except the seeking of the Face of his Lord, the Most High.'
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a chapter heading, an introductory claim, and the presentation of Quranic evidence with a rational argument. The author begins by stating the chapter topic: the 'Face' of Allah. He then reports that proponents of affirming the Face argue from scriptural sources—both Quran and hadith. He first lists Quranic verses. After quoting the first verse (Surat ar-Rahman 26-27), he interjects a rational argument that the Face cannot be identical to Allah's essence. The argument has two parts: (1) The verse adds the Face to Allah (e.g., 'Face of your Lord'), and adding a thing to itself is impossible, so the Face must be something other than the essence. (2) The phrase 'Possessor of Majesty' (ذو الجلال) is nominative in the verse, but if it described 'Lord' (which is genitive), it should be genitive (ذي الجلال). The fact that it remains nominative indicates it describes 'Face' instead, confirming that 'Face' is a distinct thing, not the Lord Himself. This shows that the Face is an attribute, not the essence. After this argument, he lists eight more verses in sequence, each explicitly mentioning the 'Face of Allah' as the object of desire or as the enduring entity. The lesson thus establishes both the textual basis for affirming the Face and a rational principle for distinguishing it from the essence.

Key Terms
وجه الله
إضافة
صفة
ذو الجلال والإكرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Face of Allah refers to His essence or a physical body part.
Correction: This passage demonstrates linguistically and rationally that the Face is an attribute distinct from the essence, not a body part.

Checkpoint
According to the second rational argument in this passage, why would 'Dhu al-Jalal' be in the genitive if it described the Lord?
A) Because the Lord is the subject of the sentence
B) Because the attribute of a genitive noun is genitive
C) Because it is a proper noun
D) Because the verse is from Surat ar-Rahman

Key Takeaway
The rational argument from the first verse establishes that the Face of Allah is a real attribute, not the essence, using linguistic and logical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Everyone upon it is perishing, and there remains the Face of your Lord', does your heart truly distinguish between what is temporary and what is eternal? Are you investing your energy in what will perish or in seeking His Face?
?
The rational proofs in this lesson show that Allah's Face is a real attribute, not a metaphor. Does your worship reflect a deep conviction that you are standing before the One whose Face is enduring and majestic, or do you sometimes treat Him as an abstract idea?
?
If the Face of Allah is described as 'Possessor of Majesty and Honor', how does that change the way you approach Him in your daily life? Are you living with a sense of awe and honor before His attribute, or have you become careless?

The Quranic Evidence for the Face of Allah
Enumeration of eight Quranic verses mentioning the Face of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize that the Quran repeatedly mentions the Face of Allah as an object of desire and as enduring, establishing it as a real attribute.

Core Idea
Eight additional Quranic verses affirm the reality of the Face of Allah.

After presenting the rational proof from the first verse, Razi lists eight more Quranic verses that explicitly mention the Face of Allah. This collection of scriptural evidence reinforces that the attribute is well-attested in revelation, leaving no room for denial.

Guided Path
1
Read through the list of verses
Read the eight verses in order. Each one is a direct quote from the Quran containing the phrase 'Face of Allah' or equivalent.
Notice the variety of contexts: perishability, turning, charity, etc.
2
Identify the common theme
All verses involve desiring, seeking, or relating to the Face of Allah. This shows that the Face is a meaningful object of worship and devotion.
3
Understand the evidentiary purpose
The sheer number of verses (eight plus the first) provides a strong inductive argument for the reality of the attribute of the Face.
This method is typical in kalam: using multiple textual proofs to establish a doctrine.

?

Six Hadiths Affirming the Face of Allah
Razi cites six prophetic hadiths mentioning the Face of Allah as evidence for its affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how six prophetic hadiths are used by Imam Razi as scriptural evidence for affirming the Face of Allah as a divine attribute.

Core Idea
The Face of Allah is affirmed through multiple authentic hadiths.

Imam Razi now turns to the prophetic tradition to support the affirmation of the Face of Allah. He presents six hadiths that mention the Face in diverse contexts—seeking refuge, supplication, fasting, honoring, and jihad. These hadiths serve as sam‘ī evidence that complements rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the six hadiths
Read the Arabic text and translation of all six hadiths to understand their contexts.
Each hadith uses 'Face of Allah' in a different practical or devotional context.
2
Identify the common theme
Notice that all hadiths treat the Face as a real entity that can be sought, honored, or a source of refuge.
This supports the literal affirmation of the attribute.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask: Why does Razi cite multiple hadiths rather than one? The answer is to establish tawātur or widespread transmission.
Multiple hadiths from different companions strengthen the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأحبار فكثيرة
الأول: ما روى ابن خزيمة عن جابر، قال: لما نزل قوله تعالى: "قل هو القادر على أن يبعث عليكم عذاباً من فوقكم" قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: أعوذ بوجهك. ثم قال: "أو من تحت أرجلكم" قال عليه السلام: أعوذ بوجهك، ثم قال: "أو يلبسكم شيعاً ويذيق بعضكم بأس بعض" قال عليه السلام: هاتان أهون وأيسر.
الثاني: روى عمار بن ياسر عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: "اللهم بعلمك الغيب وقدرتك على الخلق أحيني ما كانت الحياة خيراً لي، وتوفني إذا كانت الوفاة خيراً لي. اللهم أسألك خشيتك في الغيب والشهادة، وكلمة الحق والعدل في الغضب والرضى، وأسألك الرضا في الفقر والغنى، وأسألك نعيماً لا يتبدل، وأسألك قرة عين لا تنقطع، وأسألك الرضاء بعد القضاء، وأسألك برد العيش بعد الموت، وأسألك لذة النظر إلى وجهك، وأسألك الشوق إلى لقائك في غير ضراء مضرة ولا فتنة مضلة. اللهم زينا بزينة الإيمان، واجعلنا هداة مهتدين."
الثالث: قال عليه السلام: "من صام يوماً في سبيل الله ابتغاء وجه الله، باعد الله وجهه عن النار سبعين خريفاً."
الرابع: عن ابن عباس عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: "من استعاذ بالله فأعيذوه، ومن سألكم بوجه الله فعظموه."
الخامس: عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: "مثل المجاهد في سبيل الله ابتغاء وجه الله، مثل القائم المصلي حتى يرجع من جهاده."
السادس: قال عبد الله: قسم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال رجل: إن هذه القسمة ما أريد بها وجه الله. فأتيت...
And as for the hadiths, they are many. First: What Ibn Khuzaymah narrated from Jabir, who said: When the saying of the Exalted was revealed: 'Say: He is the One capable of sending upon you punishment from above you,' the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'I seek refuge in Your Face.' Then he said: 'Or from beneath your feet,' he, peace be upon him, said: 'I seek refuge in Your Face.' Then he said: 'Or to cover you in sects and make you taste one another's violence,' he, peace be upon him, said: 'These two are easier and lighter.' Second: Ammar ibn Yasir narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'O Allah, by Your knowledge of the unseen and Your power over creation, keep me alive as long as life is good for me, and cause me to die when death is better for me. O Allah, I ask You for fear of You in the unseen and the witnessed, and the word of truth and justice in anger and satisfaction, and I ask You for contentment in poverty and wealth, and I ask You for bliss that does not change, and I ask You for coolness of eye that does not cease, and I ask You for pleasure after decree, and I ask You for coolness of life after death, and I ask You for the delight of looking at Your Face, and I ask You for longing to meet You without harmful adversity or misleading trial. O Allah, adorn us with the adornment of faith, and make us guided guides.' Third: He, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever fasts a day in the way of Allah, seeking the Face of Allah, Allah will distance his face from the Fire by seventy autumns.' Fourth: From Ibn Abbas from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Whoever seeks refuge with Allah, then give him refuge, and whoever asks you by the Face of Allah, then honor him.' Fifth: From Abu Hurayrah, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'The example of the mujahid in the way of Allah, seeking the Face of Allah, is like the one standing in prayer until he returns from his jihad.' Sixth: Abdullah said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, divided (spoils), and a man said: 'This division was not intended for the Face of Allah.' So I came...
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a collection of six prophetic hadiths that mention the 'Face of Allah' in various contexts. The author presents them as scriptural evidence for affirming the Face as a divine reality. The first hadith shows the Prophet seeking refuge in Allah's Face when a verse about punishment was revealed, indicating the Face is a source of protection. The second is a lengthy supplication that includes asking for the 'delight of looking at Your Face,' showing that seeing the Face is a reward in the afterlife. The third hadith promises that fasting for Allah's Face distances one from the Fire. The fourth commands honoring those who ask by Allah's Face, treating the Face as a sacred means. The fifth equates the reward of jihad for the Face with continuous prayer. The sixth (incomplete) reports a complaint that a division was not done for the Face of Allah. All these hadiths demonstrate that the Face of Allah is a well-established concept in Islamic teachings, used in worship, supplication, and eschatology, thus supporting the affirmation of the Face as a real attribute.

ابتغاء وجه الله

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These hadiths are merely metaphorical and do not affirm a real attribute.
Correction: Imam Razi uses these hadiths as literal evidence to affirm the Face as a real attribute of Allah, while avoiding anthropomorphism by understanding it as a true attribute without similitude.

Three Reasons Why 'Wajh' is Metaphor for the Essence
Imam Razi gives three arguments for 'wajh' as metaphor for essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three linguistic and theological reasons that Imam Razi provides to justify the metaphorical use of 'wajh' (face) for the divine essence.

Core Idea
The face represents the entire person because it is the most visible, expressive, and functionally significant part.

Imam Razi has established that 'wajh' in the Quran refers to Allah's essence. Now he explains why this metaphorical usage is linguistically valid. Drawing on human experience, he gives three compelling reasons rooted in how the face functions as a representation of the whole person.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage and Translation
First, read the Arabic text of Imam Razi's arguments and the corresponding English translation. Try to grasp the overall structure before diving into analysis.
2
Identify the Three Reasons
Notice how Imam Razi explicitly lists three 'wujūh' (aspects or reasons). Underline each one in your mind: visibility, manifestation of faculties, and beauty/expressiveness.
The reasons are marked by 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
3
Analyze the Logical Connection
For each reason, ask: Why does this particular characteristic of the human face justify using 'wajh' for the entire essence? Understand the analogy Imam Razi is drawing.
The metaphor relies on the face being the most representative part of a person.
4
Reflect on the Linguistic Principle of Kināyah
Consider how this classical argument demonstrates the concept of kināyah (metonymy) in Arabic. The face is a part that stands for the whole due to its prominence.
This avoids any literal interpretation that would imply physicality for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : لفظ الوجه قد يجعل كناية عن الذات تارة، وعن الرضى أخرى . أما الأول فنقول : السبب في وجوب جعل الوجه كناية عن الذات وجوه : الأول : إن المرئى من الإنسان في أكثر الأوقات ليس إلا وجهه ، وبوجهه يتميز ذلك الإنسان ، عن غيره . فالوجه كأنه هو العضو الذى به يتحقق وجود ذلك الإنسان ، وبه يعرف كونه موجودا . ولما كان الأمر كذلك ، لاجرم حسن جعل الوجه اسما لكل الذات . ومما يقوى ذلك : أن القوم إذا كان معهم إنسان يرتب أحوالهم . ويقوم بإصلاح أمورهم ، سمى وجه القوم ، ووجيههم . والسبب فيه : ما ذكرنا . الثاني : إن المقصود من الإنسان ظهور آثار عقله وحسه وفهمه وفكره. ومعلوم : أن معدن هذه الأحوال هو الرأس ، ومظهر آثار هذه القوى هو الوجه . ولما كان معظم المقصود من خلق الإنسان إنما يظهر في الوجه، لاجرم حسن إطلاق اسم الوجه على كل الذات . الثالث : إن الوجه مخصوص بمزيد الحسن واللطافة ، والتركيب العجيب . والتأليف الغريب . وكل ما فى القلب من الأحوال ، فإنه يظهر على الوجه ، فلما امتاز الوجه عن سائر الأعضاء بهذه الخواص ، لاجرم حسن إطلاق لفظ الوجه على كل الذات .
If you have understood this, we say: the word 'wajh' is sometimes used as a metaphor for the essence, and other times for pleasure. As for the first, we say: the reason for the necessity of making 'wajh' a metaphor for the essence has three aspects: First: The part of a person that is seen most of the time is only his face, and by his face that person is distinguished from others. So the face is as if it is the organ by which the existence of that person is realized, and by which he is known to exist. Since this is the case, it is fitting to make the word 'wajh' a name for the entire essence. And what strengthens this is that when a group has a person who arranges their affairs and undertakes to reform their matters, they call him 'the face of the group' and 'their distinguished one'. The reason for that is what we have mentioned. Second: The purpose of a person is the manifestation of the effects of his intellect, sensation, understanding, and thought. And it is known that the source of these states is the head, and the manifestation of the effects of these faculties is the face. Since most of the purpose of creating a person appears in the face, it is fitting to apply the name 'wajh' to the entire essence. Third: The face is distinguished by additional beauty and delicacy, wondrous composition, and strange arrangement. And every state that is in the heart appears on the face. So when the face is distinguished from other limbs by these properties, it is fitting to apply the word 'wajh' to the entire essence.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage follows the previous proof. Imam Razi now explains why the word 'wajh' is used metaphorically for the divine essence. He gives three reasons: 1) The face is the most visible and identifying part of a person, so it represents the whole. This is reinforced by the linguistic usage: a leader is called 'the face of the people'. 2) The face is where intellectual and sensory functions manifest, making it the locus of human purpose. 3) The face is uniquely beautiful and expressive, reflecting inner states. These reasons justify using 'wajh' for the essence of God, who is beyond physicality, as a metonymy for His being.

Key Terms
الذات (al-dhāt)
كناية (kināyah)
الوجه (al-wajh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that calling God's essence 'wajh' implies that Allah has a physical face or body.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that 'wajh' is a linguistic metaphor (kināyah) based on how the human face represents the whole person. It is not a literal attribution of a body part to Allah.

Checkpoint
How many reasons does Imam Razi give for using 'wajh' as a metaphor for the essence?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The word 'wajh' is metaphorically applied to Allah's essence based on three linguistic parallels from human experience: visibility, functional manifestation, and unique beauty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's 'face' (wajh), do you truly envision His entire essence, or do you unconsciously imagine a physical part? How can you train your heart to transcend the physical metaphor and worship the complete, infinite essence?
?
Razi argues that the face reveals what is in the heart. If your face were to fully expose your inner spiritual state right now, what would it show about your love for Allah? What might you need to change so that your outward appearance matches a heart devoted to Him?
?
The face is called 'wajh' because it is the most expressive part. In what ways does your life 'express' your belief in Allah's attributes? Are you more concerned with what people see on your 'face' or with what Allah sees in your heart?

The Two Quranic Uses of 'Wajh' for God
Al-Razi analyzes the word 'wajh' as applied to God in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Quranic uses of the word 'wajh' for God (essence and pleasure) and the linguistic reasoning behind them, as explained by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
The word 'wajh' in the Quran can refer to God's essence or His pleasure, depending on context.

Imam al-Razi examines a key term used in the Quran for God: 'wajh' (face). He shows that it is not literal but a metaphor, used in two distinct senses. This lesson unpacks his linguistic analysis, helping you distinguish between verses where 'wajh' means God's essence and those where it means His pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice how al-Razi builds his argument step by step.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the metaphor for essence
Al-Razi first explains why 'wajh' can stand for the entire essence. The face reveals inner states, so it is a natural metaphor for the whole person—and by extension, God's essence.
Key Arabic term: الذات (al-dhat).
3
Understand the metaphor for pleasure
Next, al-Razi shows that turning one's face toward something indicates inclination, so 'wajh' can be a metaphor for divine pleasure (الرضى).
Key Arabic term: الرضى (al-rida).
4
Apply to specific Quranic verses
Al-Razi then applies this analysis to several verses. For verses like 'Everything is perishing except His Face,' 'wajh' means essence. For verses like 'We only feed you for the sake of the Face of God,' it means pleasure.
Distinguish between the two groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ما في القلب من الأحوال فإنه يظهر على الوجه فلما امتاز الوجه عن سائر الأعضاء بهذه الخواص لاجرم حسن إطلاق لفظ الوجه على كل الذات. وأما بيان السبب في جواز جعل لفظ الوجه كناية عن الرضى فهو أن الإنسان إذا مال قلبه إلى الشيء أقبل بوجهه عليه وإذا كره شيئاً أعرض بوجهه عنه فلما كان إقبال الإنسان بوجهه عليه من لوازم كونه مائلاً إليه لاجرم حسن جعل لفظ الوجه كناية عن الرضى. إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فنقول: أما قوله تعالى: كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه وقوله: ويبقى وجه ربك فالمراد منه الذات والمقصود من ذكره التأكيد والمبالغة فإنه يقال: وجه هذا الأمر كذا وكذا ووجه هذا الدليل هو كذا وكذا والمراد منه هو نفس ذلك الشيء ونفس ذلك الدليل فكذا هذا. وأما قوله تعالى: فثم وجه الله إنما نطعمكم لوجه الله إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى فالمراد من الكل رضى الله تعالى. وهكذا القول في تلك الأحاديث وبالله التوفيق
And every state that is in the heart appears on the face. So when the face was distinguished from the rest of the limbs by these properties, it was proper to apply the word 'face' to the entire essence. As for the explanation of the reason for the permissibility of making the word 'face' a metaphor for pleasure: it is that when a person's heart inclines to something, he turns his face toward it, and when he dislikes something, he turns his face away from it. So when a person's turning his face toward it is among the necessities of his being inclined to it, it was proper to make the word 'face' a metaphor for pleasure. When you have understood this introduction, we say: As for His saying, the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' and His saying: 'And there remains the Face of your Lord,' what is intended by it is the essence. And the purpose of mentioning it is emphasis and exaggeration, for it is said: 'the face of this matter is such and such' and 'the face of this proof is such and such,' and what is intended by it is the very thing itself and the very proof itself. So likewise this. As for His saying, the Exalted: 'Then there is the Face of God,' 'We only feed you for the sake of the Face of God,' 'except seeking the Face of his Lord, the Most High,' what is intended by all of it is the pleasure of God, the Exalted. And such is the statement regarding those hadiths. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis of the word 'wajh' (وجه) as applied to God in the Qur'an. Al-Razi first justifies its application to God's essence (الذات) by noting that the face is distinguished from other body parts because it reveals inner states; thus, it is permissible to metaphorically apply 'wajh' to the entire essence. He then explains why 'wajh' can be a metaphor for divine pleasure (الرضى): when a person is inclined to something, he turns his face toward it, and when he dislikes something, he turns away. Since turning the face is a necessary consequence of inclination, 'wajh' becomes a suitable metaphor for pleasure. After establishing this principle, al-Razi applies it to specific Qur'anic verses. For verses like 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (28:88) and 'There remains the Face of your Lord' (55:27), the intended meaning is God's essence. The mention of 'face' here is for emphasis, similar to idiomatic expressions like 'the face of this matter' meaning the matter itself. For verses like 'Then there is the Face of God' (2:115), 'We only feed you for the sake of the Face of God' (76:9), and 'except seeking the Face of his Lord, the Most High' (92:20), the intended meaning is God's pleasure. This same interpretation applies to relevant hadiths. The passage thus distinguishes between literal/metaphorical uses of 'wajh' in scripture, ensuring a correct understanding of God's transcendence.

Key Terms
وجه
الذات
الرضى
كناية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret 'wajh' in all Quranic verses as a literal face for God, leading to anthropomorphism.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that 'wajh' is a metaphor: in some verses it refers to God's essence (for emphasis), and in others it refers to His pleasure.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what does the word 'wajh' mean in the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face'?
A) God's pleasure
B) God's essence
C) God's face
D) God's mercy

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's analysis shows that 'wajh' in the Quran is a metaphor used either for God's essence (for emphasis) or for His pleasure, depending on the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Quranic verses that mention God's 'face', do you pause to reflect on the intended meaning (essence or pleasure) based on context? How can you train yourself to discern this?
?
Al-Razi shows that language about God is precise and metaphorical. Are there any words you use about Allah that might carry unintended anthropomorphic implications? How will you correct that?
?
The verse 'We only feed you for the sake of the Face of God' (76:9) turns an action of charity into seeking God's pleasure. In your daily actions, how often do you consciously purify your intention for the 'wajh' of Allah? What is one concrete change you can make today?

Qur'anic Evidence for God's Attribute of 'Eyes'
Al-Razi cites three Qur'anic verses to affirm the attribute of 'eyes' for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses three Qur'anic verses to affirm the attribute of 'eyes' for God in a manner of care and protection.

Core Idea
The Qur'an uses 'eyes' as a symbol of divine care, not physicality.

Al-Razi begins this chapter by presenting the scriptural foundation for affirming divine attributes. The first line of evidence comes from the Qur'an itself, where God attributes 'eyes' to Himself in the context of protecting His prophets. This lesson examines those three verses and their theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts with a general statement about the Qur'an.
2
Identify the three verses
Find each of the three Qur'anic verses cited. Notice how each one uses the word 'eye' (أعين or عيني) in a context of divine supervision.
The verses are from the stories of Noah, Moses, and Muhammad.
3
Reflect on the common theme
Ask yourself what these three verses have in common. They all affirm God's 'eyes' in relation to prophethood and divine care.
This is a key point for understanding the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع عشر في احتجوا على ثبوتها بالقرآن والأخبار. أما القرآن: فقوله تعالى لنوح عليه السلام: واصنع الفلك بأعيننا ولموسى عليه السلام: ولتصنع على عيني ولمحمد: واصبر لحكم ربك فإنك بأعيننا. وأما الأخبار: فروى صاحب شرح السنة رحمه الله في باب ذكر الدجال عن ابن عمر رضى الله عنهما قال: قام رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الناس فأثنى على الله بما هو أهله ثم ذكر الدجال فقال: "إني لأنذركموه وما من نبي إلا أنذر قومه لقد أنذر نوح قومه ولكني سأقول لكم فيه قولاً لم يقله نبي لقومه: إنه أعور وإن الله ليس بأعور" ثم قال صاحب الكتاب: هذا حديث صحيح أخرجه البخاري في كتابه. وروى أيضا عن ابن عباس رضى الله عنه أنه ذكر الدجال عند النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: "إن الله لا يخفى عليكم إنه ليس بأعور وأشار بيده إلى عينيه... وإن المسيح الدجال أعور عينه اليمنى كان عينه عنبة طافية" ثم قال: هذا حديث اتفق الشيخان على صحته. ومما يدل
Chapter Fourteen: On [the subject that] they argued for their affirmation by the Qur'an and reports. As for the Qur'an: His saying, the Exalted, to Noah, peace be upon him: 'And build the ship under Our eyes' and to Moses, peace be upon him: 'And that you be formed under My eye' and to Muhammad: 'And be patient for the judgment of your Lord, for you are under Our eyes.' As for the reports: The author of Sharh al-Sunna, may God have mercy on him, narrated in the chapter on the mention of the Dajjal, from Ibn 'Umar, may God be pleased with them both, who said: The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, stood among the people, praised God as He deserves, then mentioned the Dajjal and said, 'I warn you of him, and there is no prophet who did not warn his people. Indeed, Noah warned his people, but I will say to you a saying that no prophet said to his people: He is one-eyed, and indeed God is not one-eyed.' Then the author of the book said: This is an authentic hadith, al-Bukhari included it in his book. He also narrated from Ibn 'Abbas, may God be pleased with him, that he mentioned the Dajjal before the Prophet, peace be upon him, and he said: 'Indeed, God is not hidden from you; indeed, He is not one-eyed,' and he pointed with his hand to his eyes, '... and indeed the false messiah (al-Masih al-Dajjal) is one-eyed, his right eye is like a floating grape.' Then he said: This is a hadith whose authenticity both al-Bukhari and Muslim agreed upon. And among what indicates
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Chapter Fourteen, where the author presents scriptural evidence for affirming divine attributes (specifically the attribute of 'eyes' and the negation of defect). The chapter begins by stating that the proponents (احتجوا) rely on the Qur'an and hadith. First, three Qur'anic verses are cited that attribute 'eyes' (أعين) to God in a context of care and protection: to Noah ('under Our eyes'), to Moses ('under My eye'), and to Muhammad ('under Our eyes'). Then, two hadiths are narrated from reliable sources (Sharh al-Sunna, Bukhari and Muslim). The first hadith from Ibn 'Umar reports the Prophet's warning about the Dajjal, where he explicitly says that the Dajjal is one-eyed (أعور) but God is not one-eyed. The second hadith from Ibn 'Abbas similarly states that God is not hidden and is not one-eyed, and describes the Dajjal's eye as a floating grape. The author of the book then confirms the authenticity of these hadiths. The passage ends abruptly with 'ومما يدل' (and among what indicates), suggesting the argument continues on the next page. This lesson establishes that the attribute of 'eyes' is affirmed for God, while also negating any imperfection (like being one-eyed), following the principle of affirming what God affirms for Himself in a manner befitting His majesty.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an affirms the attribute of 'eyes' for God in specific contexts of divine care and protection, without implying physicality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you living as if you are always under God's eyes? When you are alone, do you still act with the same awareness of His watchful care as if you were in a crowd? What is one area of your private life where you need to remember that He sees you?
?
The verse to Noah came during a monumental task—building the ark. Is there a difficult task God has placed before you that requires you to rely on His supervision? How can you practically remind yourself that you are 'under His eyes' today?
?
When you face trials that test your patience—like Muhammad (peace be upon him) was commanded—do you truly believe that God's eyes are upon you with care? How might your response change if you fully internalized that divine attention is not judgment but protective love?

Prophetic Hadith: God is Not One-Eyed
Al-Razi presents two authentic hadiths that negate one-eyedness for God and affirm His attribute of 'eyes'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith about the Dajjal affirms God's attribute of 'eyes' while negating any defect or similarity to created beings.

Core Idea
The Prophet explicitly contrasts God with the Dajjal: God is not one-eyed.

After citing the Qur'an, Al-Razi turns to the second source of evidence: the hadith. The hadiths narrated about the Dajjal provide a powerful refutation of any anthropomorphic notion of God's attributes. The Prophet explicitly states that God is not one-eyed, affirming the attribute of 'eyes' for God while negating any imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith passages
Read the Arabic passages for the two hadiths. Notice the key phrase 'ليس بأعور' (is not one-eyed) that appears in both.
The first hadith is from Ibn Umar, the second from Ibn Abbas.
2
Identify the contrast
The Prophet contrasts the Dajjal (who is one-eyed) with God (who is not one-eyed). This negation of defect is crucial.
3
Understand authentication
Al-Razi notes that both hadiths are authentic, citing Bukhari and Muslim. This shows the reliability of the evidence.
The author of Sharh al-Sunna is mentioned as the source for the first hadith.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Ask yourself why this hadith is important for affirming divine attributes. It shows that God has 'eyes' in a perfect way, unlike any creature.
This connects to the broader principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Where are you trusting something that appears whole but is actually missing a crucial part? Ask Allah to expose the hidden defects in your attachments so you can rely only on Him, who is perfect in every way.

Scriptural Proofs for the Attribute 'Ayn
Razi enumerates scriptural proofs for 'Ayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural evidence (Quranic verses and hadith) that some scholars use to affirm the attribute of 'Ayn (Eye) for Allah.

Core Idea
The Qur'an and hadith mention God's 'eye'—but the real question is how to understand it.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the primary scriptural proofs cited by theologians who affirm the attribute of 'Ayn for Allah. These include three Quranic verses addressing Noah, Moses, and Muhammad, as well as two hadith reports. However, as we will see, Razi does not take these texts at face value.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the three Quranic verses and the two hadith. Identify the key phrase 'under Our eyes' and how it appears in each verse.
The hadith about Dajjal is particularly important.
2
Understand the literal proof
Recognize that proponents argue these texts literally attribute an eye to Allah. The Quranic verses use 'ayninā (Our eyes) and 'aynī (My eye). The hadith explicitly deny that Allah is one-eyed, implying He has eyes.
3
Notice additional evidence
Razi also cites a supplication ('Protect us with Your eye') and common Arabic usage ('The eye of God is upon you') as further indications.
These show the term 'ayn is used in religious and everyday language.
4
Prepare for Razi's critique
Reflect on why these proofs might be problematic. Razi will soon argue that literal interpretation leads to absurdity.
This lesson only covers the proofs; the objections come next.

آلة تلك الصنعة هي الثالث : ( إن ) (١٠) إثبات الأعين في الوجه الواحد ، قبيح . فثبت : أنه لابد من المصير إلى التأويل ، وذلك هو أن تحمل هذه الألفاظ على شدة العناية والحراسة . والوجه في حسن هذا المجاز : أن من عظمت عنايته بشيء ، وميله إليه، ورغبته فيه, كان كثير النظر إليه .
فجعل لفظ العين - التى هى آلة إذلك النظر - كناية عن شدة العناية .
-
وأما هذا الخبر الذى رويته. فمشكل ، لأن ظاهره يقتضى أن التي ما أظهر الفرق بين الإله تعالى ، وبين الدجال . يكون الدجال أعور ، وكون
الله تعالى ليس بأعور . وذلك بعيد . وخير الواحد إذا بلغ هذه الدرجة
أن يعتقد أن الكلام كان مسبوقة في ضعف المعنى ، وجب
قا مقدمة
لو ذكرت ، لزال هذا الإشكال . أليس راوى هذا الحديث هو ابن عمر ؟
Chapter Fourteen: On [the attribute of 'Ayn].

They argued for its establishment by the Qur'an and the reports.

As for the Qur'an: His saying, Exalted is He, to Noah, peace be upon him: 'And construct the ark under Our eyes' (11:37). And to Moses, peace be upon him: 'And that you be fashioned under My eye' (20:39). And to Muhammad: 'And be patient for the judgment of your Lord, for indeed you are under Our eyes' (52:48).

As for the reports: The author of Sharh al-Sunnah – may God have mercy on him – narrated in the chapter on mentioning the Dajjal, from Ibn 'Umar – may God be pleased with them both – who said: The Messenger of God stood among the people and praised God as He deserves, then mentioned the Dajjal and said: 'Indeed I warn you of him. No prophet warned his people except that he warned them. Indeed, Noah warned his people, but I will say to you about him a saying that no prophet said to his people: He is one-eyed, and indeed God is not one-eyed.' (4) Then the author of the book said: This is a sound hadith, narrated by al-Bukhari in his book. And it was also narrated from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that he mentioned the Dajjal in the presence of the Prophet, who said: 'Indeed, God is not hidden from you. Indeed, He is not one-eyed,' and he pointed with his hand to his eyes... 'And indeed the Dajjal is one-eyed in his right eye. His eye is like a floating grape.' Then he said: 'This is a hadith agreed upon by the two shaykhs [al-Bukhari and Muslim] as authentic.'

And also indicating the establishment of 'Ayn for God, Exalted is He: what is narrated in the supplications: 'Protect us with Your eye that never sleeps.' Also it is said in common usage: 'The eye of God is upon you.'

Know that the texts of the Qur'an cannot be taken according to their literal meaning for three reasons:

First: The literal meaning of His saying, Exalted is He: 'And that you be fashioned under My eye' (20:39) requires that Moses – peace be upon him – be settled upon that eye, attached to it, and above it. No rational person would say that.

Second: His saying, Exalted is He: 'And construct the ark under Our eyes' (11:37) requires that those eyes be the tool of that construction.

Third: Establishing multiple eyes on a single face is repugnant. So it is established that it is necessary to resort to interpretation (ta'wil). And that is to interpret these expressions as [indicating] intense care and protection. The justification for the goodness of this metaphor is that one whose care for something is great, whose inclination and desire for it is intense, often looks at it. Thus the word 'ayn – which is the instrument of that looking – is made a metonymy for intense care.

As for this hadith that I have narrated, it is problematic, because its literal meaning requires that it [only] manifests the difference between God, Exalted is He, and the Dajjal: the Dajjal is one-eyed and God, Exalted is He, is not one-eyed. That is far-fetched. The best option for one who has reached this level is to believe that the speech was preceded by a preamble that, if mentioned, would remove this problem. Is not the narrator of this hadith Ibn 'Umar?
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a kalam argument concerning the divine attribute 'Ayn ('eye'). It presents scriptural proofs, then raises rational objections to a literal interpretation, and finally offers a metaphorical interpretation while also addressing a problematic hadith.

The chapter opens by stating that proponents of the attribute cite both the Qur'an and prophetic reports (hadith). Three Quranic verses are quoted concerning Noah's ark, Moses' upbringing, and Muhammad's patience — each containing the phrase 'under Our eyes' or 'under My eye'. Then two hadith are cited: the first from Ibn 'Umar, where the Prophet says the Dajjal is one-eyed but God is not one-eyed; the second from Ibn 'Abbas with a similar statement. The author notes the authenticity of these hadith. Additional evidence is given from a supplication ('Protect us with Your eye') and common Arabic usage ('The eye of God is upon you').

Then the author states that these texts cannot be taken literally, giving three objections:
1. The verse about Moses being 'under My eye' would imply that Moses was physically on top of God's eye, which is absurd.
2. The verse about Noah constructing the ark 'under Our eyes' would imply that the eyes themselves were the tools of construction.
3. Attributing multiple eyes (plural 'Our eyes') to a single face is repugnant (i.e., anthropomorphic absurdity).

Therefore, the only recourse is ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation). The author explains that these expressions are metaphors for intense care and protection (shiddat al-'inaya wa al-hirasa). The basis for this metaphor is that a person who cares deeply about something looks at it frequently — thus the word 'ayn (eye), which is the instrument of looking, becomes a metonym for care.

Finally, the author addresses a problem with the hadith: its literal meaning seems to draw a comparison between God and the Dajjal by saying God is not one-eyed, which is problematic because it still implies a physical attribute. The author suggests that the hadith likely had a preamble that was omitted (perhaps explaining the metaphorical sense), and that the narrator Ibn 'Umar might have known that context. The solution is to believe that the speech was preceded by a clarifying statement that would remove the confusion.

Key Terms
العين
أعور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT cited as a proof for the attribute 'Ayn?
A) The verse 'And construct the ark under Our eyes'
B) The hadith 'Allah is not one-eyed'
C) The verse 'And We have honored the children of Adam'
D) The supplication 'Protect us with Your eye that never sleeps'

Key Takeaway
Scriptural proofs for 'Ayn include Quranic verses and authentic hadith, but these texts will be interpreted metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Prophet described God as 'not one-eyed', did you catch yourself forming a mental image of God? In that moment, what did your heart believe about Allah's nature? Are you willing to let go of any physical image you have created, even if it feels comforting?
?
You have read that Allah's 'eye' is mentioned in the Qur'an and hadith. Do you trust that Allah has told you about Himself what is true, or do you rely on your own imagination to fill in what He hasn't revealed? What will change in your prayer today because you know that Allah's 'eye' is not a physical eye but His all-encompassing knowledge and care?
?
The Prophet warned about the Dajjal being one-eyed, and he used that to negate any defect from Allah. Is there any way you inadvertently attribute human-like qualities to Allah in your thoughts? How would your relationship with Allah change if you consistently purified your concept of Him from any likeness to creation?

Rational Objections to Literal 'Ayn
Razi presents three rational objections to literal 'Ayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational objections that make a literal interpretation of 'Ayn (Eye) impossible.

Core Idea
Literal 'Ayn leads to absurdities no rational person can accept.

After presenting scriptural proofs, Razi immediately rejects a literal reading. He gives three logical arguments showing that taking the Quranic verses at face value results in nonsensical conclusions. These objections force the reader to seek a metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify each objection: the first about Moses, the second about the ark, the third about multiple eyes.
Each objection targets a specific verse or implication.
2
Understand the first objection
If Moses was 'under My eye' literally, he would be physically on top of God's eye, which is absurd.
3
Understand the second objection
If Noah built the ark 'under Our eyes' literally, the eyes would be tools for construction.
4
Understand the third objection
Attributing multiple eyes ('Our eyes') to a single face is repugnant (qabīḥ).
This appeals to aesthetic and rational coherence.
5
Conclusion: need for ta'wil
Razi concludes that literalism is impossible and interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary.
This sets up the metaphorical interpretation in the next lesson.

Metaphorical Interpretation: 'Ayn as Care and Protection
Razi provides a metaphorical interpretation of 'Ayn as care and protection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphorical interpretation of 'Ayn (Eye) as intense care and protection, and the reasoning behind this metaphor.

Core Idea
God's 'eye' is a metaphor for His care, not a physical organ.

Having rejected literalism, Razi provides the correct metaphorical interpretation. He explains that the word 'ayn is used figuratively to denote intense care and protection, based on the common human experience that we look intently at what we care about. This is a standard method in Kalam for dealing with anthropomorphic expressions.

Guided Path
1
Read the ta'wil passage
Read the Arabic where Razi says 'that is to interpret these expressions as indicating intense care and protection.'
2
Understand the basis of the metaphor
Razi explains that one whose care for something is great often looks at it. So the word 'eye' (the instrument of looking) becomes a metonym for care.
This is based on common experience.
3
Connect to the earlier verses
Apply this metaphor to the verses: 'under Our eyes' means 'under Our care and protection.'
This avoids anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the method
Razi uses a rational argument (li-) to justify the metaphor: because intense care entails frequent looking, the cause (looking) stands for the effect (care).
This is a common literary device (majaz mursal).

And also indicating the establishment of 'Ayn for God, Exalted is He: what is narrated in the supplications: 'Protect us with Your eye that never sleeps.' Also it is said in common usage: 'The eye of God is upon you.'

Know that the texts of the Qur'an cannot be taken according to their literal meaning for three reasons:

First: The literal meaning of His saying, Exalted is He: 'And that you be fashioned under My eye' (20:39) requires that Moses – peace be upon him – be settled upon that eye, attached to it, and above it. No rational person would say that.

Second: His saying, Exalted is He: 'And construct the ark under Our eyes' (11:37) requires that those eyes be the tool of that construction.

Third: Establishing multiple eyes on a single face is repugnant. So it is established that it is necessary to resort to interpretation (ta'wil). And that is to interpret these expressions as [indicating] intense care and protection. The justification for the goodness of this metaphor is that one whose care for something is great, whose inclination and desire for it is intense, often looks at it. Thus the word 'ayn – which is the instrument of that looking – is made a metonymy for intense care.

As for this hadith that I have narrated, it is problematic, because its literal meaning requires that it [only] manifests the difference between God, Exalted is He, and the Dajjal: the Dajjal is one-eyed and God, Exalted is He, is not one-eyed. That is far-fetched. The best option for one who has reached this level is to believe that the speech was preceded by a preamble that, if mentioned, would remove this problem. Is not the narrator of this hadith Ibn 'Umar?
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a kalam argument concerning the divine attribute 'Ayn ('eye'). It presents scriptural proofs, then raises rational objections to a literal interpretation, and finally offers a metaphorical interpretation while also addressing a problematic hadith.

The chapter opens by stating that proponents of the attribute cite both the Qur'an and prophetic reports (hadith). Three Quranic verses are quoted concerning Noah's ark, Moses' upbringing, and Muhammad's patience — each containing the phrase 'under Our eyes' or 'under My eye'. Then two hadith are cited: the first from Ibn 'Umar, where the Prophet says the Dajjal is one-eyed but God is not one-eyed; the second from Ibn 'Abbas with a similar statement. The author notes the authenticity of these hadith. Additional evidence is given from a supplication ('Protect us with Your eye') and common Arabic usage ('The eye of God is upon you').

Then the author states that these texts cannot be taken literally, giving three objections:
1. The verse about Moses being 'under My eye' would imply that Moses was physically on top of God's eye, which is absurd.
2. The verse about Noah constructing the ark 'under Our eyes' would imply that the eyes themselves were the tools of construction.
3. Attributing multiple eyes (plural 'Our eyes') to a single face is repugnant (i.e., anthropomorphic absurdity).

Therefore, the only recourse is ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation). The author explains that these expressions are metaphors for intense care and protection (shiddat al-'inaya wa al-hirasa). The basis for this metaphor is that a person who cares deeply about something looks at it frequently — thus the word 'ayn (eye), which is the instrument of looking, becomes a metonym for care.

Finally, the author addresses a problem with the hadith: its literal meaning seems to draw a comparison between God and the Dajjal by saying God is not one-eyed, which is problematic because it still implies a physical attribute. The author suggests that the hadith likely had a preamble that was omitted (perhaps explaining the metaphorical sense), and that the narrator Ibn 'Umar might have known that context. The solution is to believe that the speech was preceded by a clarifying statement that would remove the confusion.

الحراسة
مجاز
كناية

Key Takeaway
The attribute 'Ayn is interpreted metaphorically as Allah's intense care and protection, based on the link between looking and caring.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God's 'eye' is upon you with intense care and protection, or do you often feel overlooked and abandoned? What would change in your daily life if you internalized this truth right now?
?
When you face trials or hardships, does your heart immediately turn to the One whose care is constant and whose protection never wavers? Or do you seek solace elsewhere first? What step can you take today to make reliance on Allah's care your default response?
?
The metaphor links care to looking: your gaze follows what you care about most. Where does your gaze most frequently go—toward Allah and His commands, or toward the distractions of the world? Will you commit to shifting your focus today, even in one small way?

Addressing the Problematic Hadith on 'Ayn
Razi addresses the problematic hadith about 'Ayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'Allah is not one-eyed' is problematic for a metaphorical interpretation, and Razi's proposed resolution.

Core Idea
Even a sound hadith can be problematic if it suggests a comparison; context is key.

After providing the metaphorical interpretation, Razi acknowledges a difficulty: the hadith that explicitly says 'Allah is not one-eyed'. This seems to imply a literal understanding. Razi suggests that the hadith likely had a preamble that would clarify the metaphorical sense, and that the narrator Ibn 'Umar might have known that context.

Guided Path
1
Read the problematic hadith section
Read the Arabic where Razi says 'As for this hadith that I have narrated, it is problematic.'
2
Understand the problem
The hadith's literal meaning contrasts Allah (not one-eyed) with Dajjal (one-eyed), which still implies a physical eye. This seems to conflict with the metaphorical interpretation.
Razi finds this 'ba'īd' (far-fetched).
3
Razi's suggested solution
Razi says the best option is to believe that the hadith was preceded by a preamble that would remove the confusion. He hints that the narrator Ibn 'Umar might have omitted it.
This is a common approach in Kalam: assume missing context.
4
Reflect on the method
Note that Razi does not reject the hadith; he seeks a harmonization through hypothetical context.
This shows respect for authentic hadith while maintaining rational theology.

Chapter Fourteen: On [the attribute of 'Ayn].

They argued for its establishment by the Qur'an and the reports.

As for the Qur'an: His saying, Exalted is He, to Noah, peace be upon him: 'And construct the ark under Our eyes' (11:37). And to Moses, peace be upon him: 'And that you be fashioned under My eye' (20:39). And to Muhammad: 'And be patient for the judgment of your Lord, for indeed you are under Our eyes' (52:48).

As for the reports: The author of Sharh al-Sunnah – may God have mercy on him – narrated in the chapter on mentioning the Dajjal, from Ibn 'Umar – may God be pleased with them both – who said: The Messenger of God stood among the people and praised God as He deserves, then mentioned the Dajjal and said: 'Indeed I warn you of him. No prophet warned his people except that he warned them. Indeed, Noah warned his people, but I will say to you about him a saying that no prophet said to his people: He is one-eyed, and indeed God is not one-eyed.' (4) Then the author of the book said: This is a sound hadith, narrated by al-Bukhari in his book. And it was also narrated from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that he mentioned the Dajjal in the presence of the Prophet, who said: 'Indeed, God is not hidden from you. Indeed, He is not one-eyed,' and he pointed with his hand to his eyes... 'And indeed the Dajjal is one-eyed in his right eye. His eye is like a floating grape.' Then he said: 'This is a hadith agreed upon by the two shaykhs [al-Bukhari and Muslim] as authentic.'

And also indicating the establishment of 'Ayn for God, Exalted is He: what is narrated in the supplications: 'Protect us with Your eye that never sleeps.' Also it is said in common usage: 'The eye of God is upon you.'

Know that the texts of the Qur'an cannot be taken according to their literal meaning for three reasons:

First: The literal meaning of His saying, Exalted is He: 'And that you be fashioned under My eye' (20:39) requires that Moses – peace be upon him – be settled upon that eye, attached to it, and above it. No rational person would say that.

That is far-fetched. The best option for one who has reached this level is to believe that the speech was preceded by a preamble that, if mentioned, would remove this problem. Is not the narrator of this hadith Ibn 'Umar?
